All during Simeon the Just’s lifetime a blessing was in the Two Breads<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">The two leavened breads required for the service of Pentecost; <i>Lev.</i> 23:17.</i> and the shew-bread; the part of each one came to an olive sized bite; some of them ate and were full, some of them ate and left over. After Simeon the Just’s death the blessing was taken from the Two Breads and the shew-bread; the part of each one came only to a pea-sized bit. The decent ones refrained from taking; the gluttons stretched out their hands. It happened that a Cohen from Sepphoris took his and another’s part; he was called “pea-sized one” to this day. This is what David said<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps.</i> 71:4.</i>, <i>My God, let me escape from the hand of the evildoer, from the palm of the criminal and oppressor</i>.”
<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 39b.</i> It was stated: “Forty years before the Temple was destroyed was the Eastern light extinguished, and the shiny strip became red, and the lot of the Name came up in the left hand. They were locking the doors of the Temple hall in the evening and in the morning they found them open. Rabban Johanan ben Zakkai addressed it: ‘Temple hall, why do you frighten us? We know that in the end you will be destroyed, as it was said<sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Zach.</i> 11:1.</i>, <i>Lebanon</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple is identified with “Lebanon”, cf. <i>Targum Onqelos</i> to <i>Deut.</i>3:25; <i>Sifry Num.</i> 134, <i>Deut.</i> 28. The last source identifies the Temple Mount as “Lebanon”.</i><i>, open your doors so fire may consume your cedars.</i>’”
<sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 5, Note 77.</i> Simeon the Just served Israel as High Priest for forty years, and in the last year he told them, in this year I am going to die. <sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote">A different version in the Babli, <i>Menaḥot</i> 109b. Josephus dates the building of the Onias Temple three generations later.</i> They asked him, whom shall we appoint in your stead? He told them, there is my son Onias before you. They went and appointed Onias. His brother [Simon]<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Name added by the corrector, consistent with quote later of R. Jehudah’s statement. In the Babli, the name is Simei, a more likely name for a son of Simeon.</i> was jealous of him, he went and dressed him in an undershirt<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Šabbat</i> 16, Note 111. The spelling in the Hebrew text here is the corrector’s following the Babli, erasing the Yerushalmi spelling retained later in the parallel statement of R. Jehudah.</i> and girded him with a belt<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">The word in parentheses is the scribe’s Yerushalmi spelling, the words in brackets the corrector’s from the Babli. The form צוצל should be recognized by the dictionaries as Yerushalmi spelling.</i>. He told them, look what this one vowed to his sweetheart: when I shall serve as High Priest I shall wear your undershirt and gird myself with your belt<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Since the garments to be worn in Temple service are prescribed in <i>Ex.</i> 25, this plot presupposes an extremely ignorant High Priest.</i>. They investigated and did not find [him]<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, probably incorrect. The investigation showed that the charges were unfounded, not that Onias was not to be found.</i>. It is said, he fled to King’s Mountain, from there he fled to Alexandria, based himself there and built there an altar, over which he recited this verse<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is.</i> 16:16.</i>, <i>on that day there will be an altar for the Eternal inside the Land of Egypt.</i> Is that not an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>? See how this one who fled from prominence went to seek it at the end<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Onias preferred not to become High Priest rather than become an object of an investigation. Nevertheless he became a kind of High Priest in Egypt.</i>, if one attained it and lost it not so much more? It was stated, these are the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah said, it was not so but they appointed Simeon; his brother Onias was jealous of him, he went and dressed him in an undershirt and girded him with a belt. He told them, look what this one vowed to his sweetheart etc., as before<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">Aramaic note of the scribe; the text of the previous <i>baraita</i> has to be inserted here.</i> Is that not an argument <i>de minore ad majus?</i> See how this one who never attained prominence forced Israel to foreign worship<sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli <i>Menahot</i> 109b there is a discussion whether the illegitimate Onias Temple is classified as idolatry or not.</i>, if one attained it and lost it not so much more?

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They built him a ramp because of the Babylonians<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian tradition (Babli 66b) the misbehaving people were Alexandrians. This is rejected in the Yerushalmi.</i> who were plucking his hair and saying to him, go quickly, go quickly. The venerables of Jerusalem were accompanying him to the first hut<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The person leading the scapegoat has the obligation to go to the desert; the people accompanying him cannot leave their Šabbath domain, 2’000 cubits or 7½ stadia.</i>. There were ten huts between Jerusalem and the cliff, 90 stadia, 7½ per <i>mil</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">30 <i>stadia</i> are 1 <i>parasang</i> or 4 <i>mil..</i> The Greek <i>stadion</i> is a very badly defined measure; the reference here probably is to Egyptian measures; cf. <i>Introduction to Tractates Šabbat and Eruvin.</i><br>The huts were equally spaced, one <i>mil</i> apart. 90 <i>stadia</i> are 12 <i>mil;</i> as indicated in Mishnah 5 the people whose Sabbath domain was centered at the tenth hut could accompany him only for one <i>mil;</i> the last <i>mil</i> to the cliff he had to go by himself.</i><i>.</i><br>At each hut one tells him, here is food, here is water, and one accompanies him from hut to hut except for the last leg where one does not come with him to the crag but stands afar and observes his actions<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">30 <i>stadia</i> are 1 <i>parasang</i> or 4 <i>mil..</i> The Greek <i>stadion</i> is a very badly defined measure; the reference here probably is to Egyptian measures; cf. <i>Introduction to Tractates Šabbat and Eruvin.</i><br>The huts were equally spaced, one <i>mil</i> apart. 90 <i>stadia</i> are 12 <i>mil;</i> as indicated in Mishnah 5 the people whose Sabbath domain was centered at the tenth hut could accompany him only for one <i>mil;</i> the last <i>mil</i> to the cliff he had to go by himself.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There are Tannaim who state, he compressed his body<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote">The verb כבש means “to compress” (also to preserve edibles by vinegar or extended cooking, processes which shrink the volume); the noun כבש means “a ramp” (“preserve” is כבוש). It is left undecided whether the Mishnaic expression is to be read as a verb, that the man accompanying the scapegoat has to make himself inconspicuous, or as a noun, as translated in Mishnah 4.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, a genuine ramp.
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Joseph said, the Alexandrians were saying, until when do you attribute the misconduct to us<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babylonian tradition (Babli 66b) the misbehaving people were Alexandrians. This is rejected in the Yerushalmi.</i>?
“The venerables of Jerusalem were accompanying him to the first hut<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote">The person leading the scapegoat has the obligation to go to the desert; the people accompanying him cannot leave their Šabbath domain, 2’000 cubits or 7½ stadia.</i>.” It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 3:13, Babli 67a.</i>. “Ten huts within twelve <i>mil,</i> the words of Rebbi Meïr. Rebbi Jehudah says, ten<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In the parallel sources: Nine. There is no reason to adapt the Yerushalmi to the Babli sources. If each hut is at distance 1 <i>mil</i> from the preceding, the last hut is at <i>mil</i>10, from which it is another two <i>mil</i> to the crag. In the reading of the Babli, the distance of the crag from the wall of Jerusalem would only be 11 <i>mil</i> for R. Jehudah.</i> huts within ten <i>mil.</i> Rebbi Yose said, five [and all of them by eruv;]<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from the Babli, probably incorrect, since in the Yerushalmi version R. Yose accepts that his son’s R. Eliezer’s (should be R. Eleazar) argument parallels his own.</i> and so my son Eliezer said to me, I can make them five by <i>eruv</i><sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">If one does not accept the corrector’s addition, the huts postulated by R. Yose are at distances 2, 4, 6, 8, 10. The venerables of Jerusalem go halfway to the first hut, where they are met by people from the first hut who will accompany the scapegoat halfway to the second hut, etc., and the people of the fifth hut will stop one <i>mil</i> from the crag, as described in the Mishnah. In the version of R. Eliezer (Eleazar) ben R. Yose (in the Tosephta, R. Yose ben R. Jehudah), the venerables of Jerusalem make an <i>eruv</i> outside the city walls which allows them to accompany the scapegoat for two <i>mil.</i> At the end of these two <i>mil</i> they are met by people from the first hut who made an <i>eruv</i> halfway to the meeting point; they accompany to the first hut which is at distance 4 from the city wall. Another group of people who made an <i>eruv</i> in the opposite direction accompany the scapegoat halfway to the next hut which is at distance 8. In this version, the person leading the scapegoat has to walk the last two <i>mil</i> alone since the second group of people from the second hut have to stop at <i>mil</i> 10.</i>.
“At each hut one tells him, here is food, here is water.” In order to strengthen him. Why? For the evil inclination desires only what is forbidden to him. As the following: Rebbi Mana went to visit Rebbi Haggai who was weak<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">On the Day of Atonement.</i>. He told him, I am thirsty; he answered him, drink<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">R. Haggai was very old at this moment, having been a student of R. Zeˋira, the teacher of R. Jeremiah, the teacher of R. Jonah, father and teacher of R. Mana.</i>! He left him and went away. After an hour he visited him and asked him, how is your thirst? He told him, the moment you permitted me it went away. Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba told the following case: A man was walking in public<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">On the Day of Atonement.</i> with his daughter. His daughter said to him, my father, I am thirsty. He said to her, wait a little bit. She said to him, my father, I am thirsty. He said to her, wait a little bit, and she died. When Rebbi Aḥa finished the <i>musaf</i> prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">On the Day of Atonement.</i> he said before them<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">His congregants.</i>, our brothers, he who has small children should leave because of them<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">To feed them and give them to drink. Since little children are not required to fast, permitting them to drink would not quell their thirst; they actually have to be given to drink.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> What did he do? He split the shiny strip<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Which was tied to the scapegoat’s horns, Mishnah 4:2. It was expected to turn white as a sign of divine forgiveness, <i>Is.</i>1:18.</i>; half of it he bound on the rock and half of it he bound between its horns. Then he pushed it<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The scapegoat.</i> backwards, it rolled descending. It did not reach half of the declivity before it dissolved into limbs. He goes and sits under the last hut<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">Since he left his Sabbath domain by biblical commandment, he was permitted to return the 4’000 cubits to the first hut where he could rest for the remainder of the day and found food to break the fast at nightfall.</i> until nightfall. From when on does he make garments impure<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:26.</i>? From when he exits the wall of Jerusalem; Rebbi Simeon says, from when he pushes on the crag.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from <i>Šabbat</i> 9, Notes 119–121, not in the original text. Cf. Babli 67a, <i>Roš Haššanah</i> 31b.</i>[Originally they were tying it to their windows; some of them were turning white and some turning red; these were ashamed in front of the others. They changed and tied it to the door of the Sanctuary. Some years it was turning white, in others turning red. They changed and tied it to the rock.
It is written<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is.</i> 1:18.</i>, <i>let us go and argue,</i> etc. It was stated, Rebbi Eliezer said, <i>If your sins were like years</i><sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">Reading שָׁנִים as “years”, not “purple wool”. Both the distance of Heaven from Earth and the combined lifetime of the patriarchs were 500 years, cf. <i>Berakhot</i> 1:1, Note 83.</i>, etc., like the years from Heaven to Earth, <i>they will be snow white;</i> more than that, <i>they shall be like wool.</i> Rebbi Joshua said, <i>if your sins were like years,</i> like the years of the patriarchs, <i>they will be snow white;</i> more than that, <i>they shall be like wool.</i> Rebbi Yuda bar Pazi said, <i>if your sins were like years they will be snow white,</i> the first time, <i>if they were like purple they shall be like wool,</i> the second time. But the rabbis said, If the sins of a person are like his years, <i>they will be snow white</i>.]

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He<sup class="footnote-marker">97*</sup><i class="footnote">The High Priest.</i> comes to the bull and the he-goat to be burned, cut them open and removed the inner parts<sup class="footnote-marker">98*</sup><i class="footnote">Greek μηρία, τά, “thigh bones”, the parts of a sacrifice which have to be burned on the altar.</i>, put then into a basin and burned them on the altar. He bound them to bars and took them out to the place of burning. When do they make garments impure<sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:28. It is implied that other priests transport the carcasses to be burned.</i>? From the moment they leave the wall of the Temple courtyard; Rebbi Simeon says, from when the fire ignites most of them.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> [He comes to his bull, etc.]<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Addition by the corrector, slightly misquoted (Mishnah 3:9 instead of 6:7).</i> We have stated: “He comes to the bull and the he-goat to be burned.” There are Tannaim who state, the High Priest comes to read<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The public Torah reading which according to Mishnah 7:1 follows the completion of the service of purification.</i>. Rebbi Ḥananiah said, the verse supports our Mishnah: <i>He who sends the he-goat to Azazel shall wash his garments</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:26.</i><i>.</i> What is written next? <i>And the purification bull and the purification he-goat</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:27. The sacrifices have to be burned outside the sacred domain.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Mana said, it may be interpreted even following the Tanna of the <i>baraita.</i> If a person moves from Tiberias to Sepphoris, while he still is in Tiberias do people say, he dwells in Sepphoris<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">All the verse indicates is that the he-goat has to be sent away before the purification sacrifices are removed from the sacred precinct; no exact time frame is given in view of the fact that the Torah reading is not biblically prescribed at this point.</i>.
Rebbi Zeriqan said that Rebbi Zeˋira asked: If bulls and he-goats to be burned became impure, may they be burned according to their rules<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">If the carcasses are pure, then they have to be burned on the Day of Atonement by biblical decree, <i>Lev.</i> 16:27, and by practice of the Temple service at a place different from that where disabled sacrifices are usually burned (Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 12:5). If in impurity they follow the rules of disabled sacrifices, they can be burned only in the following night and at a different place.</i>? What is his problem? If they became impure before the blood was poured, but if they became impure after the blood was poured<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Then the sacrifice is valid and does not have to be repeated even if some subsequent action would have been required which now becomes impossible.</i> I am applying to it, <i>at its appointed time</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Num.</i> 9, 2 28:2. <i>Sifry Num.</i> 65, 142; <i>Mekhilta Ba</i> 5 (ed. Horovitz-Rabin p. 17), Tosephta <i>Pisha</i> 4:14; <i>Pesaḥim</i> 6:1 Notes 9, 10, Babli <i>Pesaḥim</i> 66a.</i>, even on the Sabbath, <i>at its appointed time,</i> even in impurity. [Rebbi Yose said, there where it is written <i>at its appointed time;</i> you do not infer anything from it<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Since in <i>Lev.</i> 16 neither the expression “at its appointed time” nor an equivalent is mentioned, the argument simply does not apply in our case.</i>.]<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of the corrector, in good Yerushalmi language. The addition is necessary to understand R. Mana’s remark and must have been taken from a manuscript source.</i> Rebbi Mana said, does not Rebbi Yose refrain from applying it here? He applies it to the bull of the Anointed and the Congregation<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 4:1–21. Since these sacrifices can only be brought on a weekday, the only problem is that of the place of burning as given in Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 12:5. He will accept that they not be burned on the appointed place only if they become invalid by a cause other than impurity.</i> {he said to him}<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">It seems that this has to be deleted.</i>.
Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, Rebbi Eleazar asked: If of bulls and he-goats to be burned a limb left which completes a majority<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 104b. Since it was stated in Mishnah 7 that the carcasses become a source of severe impurity once they are brought outside the enclosure of the Temple courtyards, the question is the determination of the exact point in time when this happens. As stated in the next sentence, it is agreed that a majority of the carcass must have left. The question remains whether the majority is counted atom by atom, which would be impossible to determine exactly, or limb by limb. Since the only practical solution is to count limb by limb, must the limb which completes the majority be totally outside or possibly only partially.</i>? His question implies that the matter depends on a majority. The following is obvious: If most of it left and after this a limb left which completes a majority. [This must be: It left limb by limb and after this a limb left which completes a majority<sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding sentence in fact makes no sense.</i>..]<sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of the corrector, in good Yerushalmi language. They must have been taken from a manuscript source.</i>
Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, Rebbi Eleazar asked: If bulls and he-goats to be burned were taken out and brought back<sup class="footnote-marker">118</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was established in the previous paragraph that the carcasses are the cause of severe impurity by biblical decree once the majority of the limbs was removed from the Temple precinct, the question necessarily refers to single limbs which do not constitute a majority of the carcass. The question is different in the Babli, <i>Zevaḥim</i>105a.</i>? It is obvious for us that in returning they do not make garments impure<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">A limb even partially taken out of the sacred precinct becomes disqualified. Even if a person other than the carrier who touches a limb while it is taken back may become impure in his person, the impurity does not extend to his clothing since the biblical conditions for this are not satisfied.</i>. What our problem is, do they make garments impure from the moment they started to be brought out<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">As will be seen, the question is about people other than the carriers of the carcass or its limbs. If the carcass was partially taken out but then returned, and a third party touched the body, do his garments become impure?</i>? Rebbi Abba objected, did we not state<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 12:6.</i>: “They were carrying them on yokes. If the front carriers left the walls of the Temple courtyard while the back carriers did not yet leave, the front carriers make garments impure while the back carriers do not make garments impure until they also leave. After they left, these and those make garments impure.” Rebbi Yudan, Rebbi Mattaniah’s father, said, there is a difference, for it is written, <i>then he shall take out</i><sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 4:12, about the purification sacrifice of the anointed priest.</i><i>,</i> until he completely leave the domain from which it is taken out<sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote">The carriers become impure the moment they are completely outside the wall of the Temple courtyard, but the carcass becomes a source of severe impurity for any who touch it only after it was completely taken out of the sacred domain. The carriers become impure even if they never come in contact with the carcass. The wooden bars on which the carcass is carried do not transmit impurity by touch; the impurity of the carriers is induced by the motion of the carcass, not by contact.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 104a.</i> Rebbi Jeremiah said, Rebbi Eleazar asked: If bulls and he-goats to be burned became impure, may they still become disqualified for being taken out<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">If the carcasses become impure inside the sacred precinct they automatically are disqualified. Once disqualified they cannot become more disqualified by being taken out. Either with the classical commentaries one has to delete the words “became impure” and (with the Babli) ask whether bulls and he-goats to be burned, for which being taken outside is a biblical commandment at a later stage, can become disqualified by being taken out in a earlier stage, or one restricts the question to the sacrifices of the Day of Atonement which as public sacrifices may be offered in impurity if most of Israel are impure.</i>? What is his problem? Following Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish; but following Rebbi Joḥanan it is obvious for him. For they disagreed: <sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Menaḥot</i> 7:3.</i>“If somebody slaughters a thanksgiving sacrifice inside while its bread<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">A thanksgiving offering has to be brought <i>on</i> loaves of leavened bread (<i>Lev.</i>7:13). This is interpreted to mean that the loaves have to be nearby. The question is the definition of “nearby”.</i> is outside the walls, the bread was not sanctified.” Rebbi Joḥanan said, outside the walls of Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">Since the thanksgiving sacrifice as a family celebration may be consumed anywhere within the walls of Jerusalem (which takes the place of the camp of the Israelites in the desert), one only requires that the bread be at a place where it may be consumed.</i>; Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, outside the walls of the Temple court.<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote">He requires that the bread be inside the sacred precinct since the presentation of the sacrifice is possible only there.</i> Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish is consequent in his opinion since Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, meat of well-being offerings which was taken out and then returned, if the blood is poured for it already it is disqualified because of removal<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Well-being sacrifices in general may be eaten in the entire city of Jerusalem (Mishnah <i>Zevaḥim</i> 5:7). Nevertheless, meat taken out of the sacred precinct into the city before its time (i. e., before pouring the blood on the altar walls) disqualifies the entire sacrifice. The same argument can be applied to the case under discussion.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, he questions even according to Rebbi Joḥanan. While Jerusalem is not an enclosure for most holy sacrifices, it is an enclosure for simple sacrifice. Outside the walls of Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">Where the bulls and he-goats to be burned have to be brought.</i> it is an enclosure neither for most holy sacrifices nor for simple sacrifices. Rebbi Mana said, it is not an enclosure for them<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">R. Joḥanan qualifies the thanksgiving offering if all parts are in the place of their final destination at all times. If this argument is applied to bulls and he-goats to be burned, the walls of Jerusalem become irrelevant.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar asked, do bulls and he-goats to be burned cause impurity of garments<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">One has to delete this and replace it with “of foodstuffs”. Garments become impure only from sources of severe impurity, but foodstuffs become impure even from impurity which is not transmissible to human bodies or garments. Since the question is asked whether the carcasses cause impurity even before they are taken out, there cannot be any question of severe impurity at this point in time. Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 105a.</i> without preparation<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">Foodstuffs in general can become impure only after “preparation” by contact with water; cf. <i>Terumot</i> 1:1 Note 7. Since the basis of this rule is <i>Lev.</i> 11:38 referring to grain which cannot become severely impure, it is concluded that severe impurity makes foodstuffs impure even without preparation.</i> and without impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">The carcasses of bulls and he-goats to be burned, as well as the carcass of the Red Cow, cause severe impurity to the people occupied with it but they themselves are pure; Mishnah <i>Parah</i> 8:3, Babli <i>Zebaḥim</i>105a..</i> since at the end they become sources of severe impurity? Samuel from Cappadocia objected, then they should make their parts<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">The inner parts (of the bulls of the Anointed Priest and the Congregation) which have to be burned on the altar; cf. Chapter 5 Note 155.</i> impure. So only after they were taken away. And even if you are saying before they were taken away, parallel to the following: purifying water does not make anything impure to make itself impure in return<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">The person who purifies another by sprinkling him with water containing ashes of the Red Cow becomes impure (<i>Num.</i>19:21) but the water itself remains pure in his hands, while in general an impure person makes water which he touches impure in the first degree.</i>. Rebbi Jeremiah objected, does not a carcass of a pure bird make food impure without preparation, without impurity, since at the end it becomes a source of severe impurity<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Tahorot</i> 1:1; <i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 11(5–6). It is inferred that <i>Lev</i>. 17:15 must refer to meat that only is forbidden because it is carcass meat, and that the verse cannot refer to four-legged animals since they are impure from the start, and it cannot refer to non-kosher animals or birds since for them the reference to tom meat would be irrelevant. The only meat which causes impurity only if taken into the mouth to be eaten is that of pure birds not correctly slaughtered. Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 105a, <i>Ḥulin</i>121a.</i>? Rebbi Yose said, the carcass of a pure bird has no enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">It is forbidden to be eaten anywhere, whereas <i>sancta</i> may be treated only in the sacred precinct.</i>; these have an enclosure. Rebbi Mana said, the carcass of a pure bird has an enclosure; the human is its enclosure<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">Only the human mouth causes impurity from carcass meat of pure birds.</i>. If it were not so, if he brought a dog, clothed it, and fed it the carcass of a pure bird, would it not make the garments impure in its palate<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">But since a living animal cannot become impure, textiles worn by a dog eating the carcass of a pure bird remain pure.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar the Southerner said, the enclosure of the carcass of a pure bird is everywhere; the enclosure of those is outside of Jerusalem<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Pesaḥim</i> 5, Note 67. Pouring the blood on the altar is qualified as “eating by the altar.” Cf. Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 104b.</i>.
Rebbi Eleazar said, bulls and he-goats to be burned which he slaughtered with the intent to pour the blood the next day, he made <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Pesaḥim</i> 5, Note 67. Pouring the blood on the altar is qualified as “eating by the altar.” Cf. Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 104b.</i><i>.</i> To burn the parts on the next day he made <i>piggul</i><sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote">Parts burned on the altar are “eaten” by the altar.</i><i>.</i> To burn the meat the next day he did not make <i>piggul</i> since he neither intended it as food for humans nor as food for the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Since the carcass has to be burned outside the sacred precinct and outside of Jerusalem, the places were the parts and the meat of well-being offerings would have to be consumed, the rules of <i>piggul</i> cannot apply.</i>.
Rebbi Shammai asked, if the parts were lost, may one pour the blood for the meat? He had not heard what Rebbi Eleazar had said, since he neither intended as food for humans nor as food for the altar<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">While for other sacrifices one may pour the blood to permit the meat to be eaten by the owners (for family sacrifices), or by the Cohanim (for purification and reparation sacrifices), or by the altar (for elevation sacrifices), in the case of bulls and he-goats to be burned there is nothing to be permitted and therefore the blood may not be poured.</i>. Rebbi Zakkai stated:A non-Cohen who burned makes garments impure<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">While the carcasses must be carried out by Cohanim since non-Cohanim cannot enter the inner courtyard where the ceremonies take place, there is nothing in the verses (<i>Lev.</i> 4:12,21; 16:28) to indicate that the burning outside the camp needs Cohanim.</i>. One only has to ask, is it qualified during nighttime<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">It is clear from the verses quoted that the carcasses have to be taken out of the camp (Jerusalem) on the day of the sacrifice; but there are no indications that the burning has to be finished on the same day.</i>? It is an argument <i>de minore ad majus</i>. Since burning of the parts which is not qualified for a non-Cohen may be done in the night, for these<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Bulls and he-goats to be burned.</i> which are qualified for a non-Cohen it is logical that it should be qualified during nighttime.
<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">Bulls and he-goats to be burned.</i><i>And</i> <i>he who burns</i><sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:28.</i><i>.</i> Not the one who starts the fire, and not the one who prepares the stake. Who is <i>he who burns</i>? That is the one who helps during the burning. [Rebbi Yose said, this implies that the one who helps during the burning]<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition.</i> makes his garments impure. Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Eleazar: One who turns over an olive-sized piece<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Less than this amount is not considered “consumed”.</i> makes his garments impure. The problem is only one who stands inside<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">The Temple domain, or possibly the city of Jerusalem.</i> and in his hand is a beam with which he turns over an olive-sized piece, [what is the rule]<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Unnecessary corrector’s addition.</i>? Let us hear from the following: <i>And he shall take out, and he shall burn</i><sup class="footnote-marker">154</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 4:12, about the Anointed Priest’s bull.</i><i>.</i> Since one who takes out, only after he took to the outside, also he who burns only if he burns outside<sup class="footnote-marker">155</sup><i class="footnote">Since the two expressions are written in the same verse.</i>. There<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">About burning the carcass of the Red Cow.</i> Ḥizqiah said, <i>he shall be impure until the evening</i><sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Num.</i> 19:7.</i><i>;</i> to include him who burns<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the verse speaks only of the Cohen who directs the ceremony, all his helpers are included.</i>. Here it is the same<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">About bulls and he-goats to be burned; <i>Sifry Num.</i> 124.</i>.

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> They told the High Priest that the he-goat arrived in the desert. And from where did they know that the he-goat arrived in the desert? They had overseers<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">Greek διάδοχοι, οἱ.</i> posted who waved sheets and they knew that the he-goat had arrived in the desert.<br>Rebbi Jehudah said, did they not have an important sign? There are three <i>mil</i> from Jerusalem to Bet Horon<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Even though this text is confirmed by Maimonides’s autograph Mishnah, it seems impossible since Bet Horon is NE of Jerusalem in inhabited territory. The Babli’s reading, <i>Bet Hidud</i> seems more appropriate; it may designate <i>Wadi al-Hoḏ.</i> R. Jehudah does not require the he-goat to be on the crag, only outside agricultural land.</i>; they were walking one <i>mil,</i> returning one <i>mil,</i> and waiting for another <i>mil;</i> then they knew that the he-goat had arrived in the desert. Rebbi Ismael said, did they not have another sign? A shiny strip was tied to the door of the Temple hall; when the he-goat arrived in the desert it turned white<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">He disputes the fact that the strip was no longer tied to the Temple door.</i>, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:26.</i>, <i>if your sins were like crimson they will be white like snow.</i>
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> What are διάδοχοι? Ones opposite one another<sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">They were stationed so that each one could see the preceding and the following ones.</i>.

Chapter 7



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The High Priest comes to read. If he wants to read in the byssus garments<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">The white garment which he wore for the ceremonies in the Temple building.</i> he may read; otherwise in his own while stole<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">Greek στολή, ἡ.</i>. The beadle of the synagogue<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">The synagogue on the Temple Mount, outside the Temple precinct. The president of the Synagogue had to carry the scroll into the Temple courtyard. This clearly is a Pharisaic institution.</i> takes the Torah scroll and hands it to the president of the synagogue, the president of the synagogue hands it to the executive officer of the Temple, the executive officer of the Temple hands it to the High Priest. The High Priest receives it standing, and reads standing. He reads <i>after the death</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:1–34.</i> and <i>but on the tenth day</i><sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i>23:26–32.</i><i>,</i> rolls up the Torah, keeps it in his bosom, and says: More than what I read before you is written here. <i>On the tenth day</i> in Numbers<sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Num.</i> 19:7–11.</i> he recites<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">קורא always means to recite with the correct masoretic accents.</i> by heart, and pronounces eight benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">These will be detailed in the Halakhah.</i>: For the Torah, for the Service, for thanksgiving, for the remission of sin, for the Temple, for Israel, for the priests, and general prayer.<br>He who sees the High Priest reading does not see the burning of the bull and the ram; he who sees the burning of the bull and the ram does not see the High Priest reading. Not because he would not be entitled to it but because the distance was large and the actions happened simultaneously.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “The High Priest comes,” etc. From where the reading of the Chapter<sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">But there is no claim that reading of any text other than <i>Lev.</i> 16 is a biblical commandment.</i>? Rebbi Idi in the name of Rebbi Isaac<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">The two Medieval quotes of this passage, <i>Tosaphot Yeshenim</i> and Ritba, read R. Joḥanan. This was the scribe’s original text corrected by himself to: R. Isaac.</i>: <i>And he executed</i>, what does the verse say? <i>As the Eternal had commanded Moses</i><sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:34.</i>, from here the reading of the Chapter<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">Since the commandment to Moses was verbal, so the execution had to be verbal. The Babli 5b has a similar argument concerning the initiation rites of the Cohanim.</i>.
Rebbi Immi in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Ancillary services<sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote">. Actions either such as reading the Torah which is not absolutely necessary, in that its omission does not invalidate the service, or necessary actions which may be performed by a non-Cohen, such as slaughter of sacrifices.</i> are performed in white garments<sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">But not necessarily in linen garments as prescribed in <i>Lev.</i> 16:4 for all service performed inside the sanctuary.</i>. Rebbi Mattanah said, the Mishnah implies this: “otherwise in his own white stole.” Rebbi Yose asked: Why? Even though they are outside? Is not slaughter only outside and is performed in golden garments<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">The Mishnah (3:5, 7:3,6) prescribes that the daily and holiday sacrifices are offered by the High Priest in his golden garments in which he serves all other days of the year.</i>? Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If he wants, he performs in golden garments, if he wants, he performs in white garments. We find that slaughter<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">While the High Priest must wear his everyday garments (Mishnah 7:7) for all priestly actions required for daily and holiday sacrifices, this is not directly prescribed for slaughter which is not a priestly duty.</i>, if he wants, he performs in golden garments, if he wants, he performs in white garments.
<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah the text is also in <i>Sotah</i> 7:6, Notes 190–211, <b>ס</b>..</i> Everywhere you are saying, one goes to the Torah. But here you are saying, one brings the Torah to them. Only since they are important personalities, the Torah is honored by them<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli 69a, the Torah scroll is brought by a chain of increasingly important people to stress the exalted position of the High Priest. Cf. Note 190.</i>. Does one not bring there the Torah to the Head of the Diaspora<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">The claim of the Heads of the Diaspora to Davidic descent may be questionable, but since it is accepted by the people, the Head of the Diaspora may claim Davidic prerogatives.</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun said, there since the seed of David is absorbed in them<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is also in <i>Megillah</i> 4:5 (75b l. 52), <b>ל</b>. Quoted in <i>Tosaphot Yeshenim</i>70a.</i>, they follow the usage of their forefathers.
<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph is also in <i>Megillah</i> 4:5 (75b l. 52), <b>ל</b>. Quoted in <i>Tosaphot Yeshenim</i>70a.</i> There<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Megillah</i> 4:5.</i>, we have stated: “One skips in prophets; one does not skip in the Torah.<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">Public readings of the Torah must present a continuous text. Readings from the Prophets may be pieced together, such as in the Ashkenazic Haftara to Yitro, <i>Is</i>. 6:1–7:6, 9:5–6.</i>” “One skips in prophets,” but one does not skip from one prophet to another, except that one is permitted to skip between the Twelve Prophets<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">The 12 minor prophets are considered one book. The Ashkenazic Haftara for the Sabbath between New Year’s day and Yom Kippur is composed of <i>Hos</i>. 14:2–10 and either <i>Joel</i> 2:15–27 or <i>Micah</i>7:18–20.</i>. “One does not skip in the Torah.” Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish: Because one does not scroll the book of the Torah in public. Rebbi Yose asked, think of it, if it was a small paragraph<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">If one continues on the same page after an interruption, nothing has to be scrolled.</i>? But so that Israel should hear the Torah in due order. But did we not state: “he reads <i>after the death</i><sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:1–34.</i> and <i>but on the tenth day</i>”<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i>23:26–32.</i>? There is a difference since this is the order of the day. You should know this, as Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, one never recites from memory but here he recites<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The sacrifices detailed in <i>Num</i>.19:7–11 (offered by the High Priest in his year-round “golden” garments) are completely different from those required by <i>Lev.</i> 16, for whose service white linen garments are prescribed. Therefore mention of the text in <i>Num.</i> 19 is essential, but scrolling such a large distance would be an interruption of the public service.</i>. Rebbi Yose ordered Bar Ulla, the beadle of the synagogue of the Babylonians: If there is one Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote">On a day which requires reading two different sections, such as a New Moon celebrated on a Sabbath, or a holiday. Then the Torah has to be scrolled, but this should not be done in public.</i>, scroll it behind a curtain. If there are two, take away one and bring the other<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">This is current Sephardic practice, in contrast to current Ashkenazic procedure where the second Torah is brought before the first is lifted.</i>.
“And pronounces eight benedictions<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">These will be detailed in the Halakhah.</i>: For the Torah”, He Who chooses the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli 70a and Tosephta 3:18, “in the way one recites the benediction for the Torah in the synagogue.” This is understood also here for this and the following benedictions, in the Galilean versions.</i>. “For the Service,” for You we fear and serve<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">The Galilean version of the 16th benediction of the <i>Amidah</i>.</i>. “For thanksgiving,” it is good to give thanks to You<sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote">The 17th benediction.</i>, “for the remission of sin,” He who remits the sins of His people Israel.<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">The final paragraph of the middle benediction in the <i>Amidah</i> for the Day of Atonement.</i> “For the Temple,” He Who chooses the Temple. Rebbi Idi said, He Who dwells in Zion<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">The full text of this benediction is unknown. Since R. Idi is quoted as commenting, not disagreeing, one has to assume that he quotes the beginning of the relevant prayer text.</i>. “For Israel,” He Who chooses Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">In the opinion of I. Elbogen this refers to the benediction immediately preceding the recitation of <i>Shema</i>‘.</i>, “for the priests.” “And general prayer.”, entreaty and begging, for Your people Israel need to be helped before You. Praise to You, Eternal, Who hears prayer<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">This is the only prayer text which is unique to the High Priest’s reading.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If he read in his byssus garments, he sanctified hands and legs,<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote">But if he read in his own stole he already had to wash hands and feet when he took off his white garments (Chapter 3, Note 98). Dressing and undressing profane garments does not necessitate washing hands and feet.</i> and undressed, stepped down, and immersed himself, came up, and dried himself with a towel. They brought him the golden garments<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The High Priest’s garments for service all year long. These are worn for all parts of all year long. These are worn for all parts of the service of the Day of Atonement which do not involve entering the Holiest of Holies.</i>, he dressed and sanctified hands and legs.<br>He went out and sacrificed his ram, and the people’s ram<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">As prescribed by <i>Lev.</i> 16:24. The biblical expression “did” is read to imply that the entire work involved, slaughter, dismembering, cleaning, transporting to the altar, and burning the elevation sacrifices, had to be performed by the unaided High Priest.</i>, and the seven unblemished sheep<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">As prescribed by <i>Num.</i> 29:8.</i>, the words of Rebbi Eliezer. Rebbi Aqiba says, they<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">The animals enumerated by R. Eliezer were brought before the High Priest wore the white garments for the first time.</i> had been brought with the daily morning offering; the bull of elevation offering and the he-goat offered outside were brought with the daily afternoon sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">This statement is the common opinion of R. Eliezer and R. Aqiba. These sacrifices are <i>musaf</i> sacrifices prescribed in <i>Num.</i> 29:8</i>. He sanctified hands and legs and undressed, stepped down, and immersed himself, came up and dried himself with a towel. They brought him the white garments<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">. To re-enter the Holiest of Holies.</i>, he dressed and sanctified hands and legs.<br>He entered to remove the cup and the pan<sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">Which were left in the Holiest of Holies. Chapter 3, Mishnah 1.</i>. He sanctified hands and legs and undressed, stepped down, and immersed himself, came up and dried himself with a towel. They brought him the golden garments, he dressed and sanctified hands and legs.<br>He entered, burned the incense, and prepared the lights. He sanctified hands and legs, and undressed, stepped down, and immersed himself, came up and dried himself with a towel. They brought him his own garments in which he dressed. One accompanies him to his house. He made a festivity for his friends when he exited in peace<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">He exited the Holiest of Holies, which is most dangerous; cf. Chapter 1, Notes 220–222.</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are the words of Rebbi Eliezer and Rebbi Aqiba. But the words of the Sages, all of them were brought with the daily morning offering<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Since the same <i>musaf</i> offering is prescribed in <i>Num.</i> 29 for New Year’s day, the Day of Atonement, and the Eighth Day of the Fall Festival, they are offered more frequently than the special offerings of the day of Atonement detailed in <i>Lev.</i> 16. Since the House of Hillel strictly follow the rule that the more frequent has precedence (cf. <i>Berakhot</i> 8:1), all of the more common sacrifices must be processed first. Cf. Tosephta 2:19.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna in the name of Rav Joseph: Rebbi Eliezer follows the House of Shammai. Just as the House of Shammai say, frequent and holy, holy has precedence<sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Nazir</i> 7:1 Note 54; Babli <i>Zevaḥim</i> 69a.</i>, so Rebbi Eliezer says, frequent and holy, holy has precedence. How is this? The service of the Day is holier and the day’s <i>musaf</i> sacrifices are more frequent; the service of the Day has precedence over the day’s <i>musaf</i> sacrifices.
The bull, the ram, and the seven unblemished sheep were brought with the daily morning offering; the remainder were brought with the daily afternoon offering<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">This is the rule of R. Aqiba.</i>. Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish said, the reason of this Tanna is to join purification sacrifice to purification sacrifice and elevation sacrifice to elevation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">Even though the he-goat as purification sacrifice is prescribed for all holidays and therefore is most frequent, as noted later in <i>Num.</i> 29:11 it is required that this sacrifice follow that of the he-goat whose blood was brought into the Temple, and be followed by the elevation sacrifice which is the evening daily offering. Therefore it makes sense to require the elevation offerings required by <i>Lev.</i> 16:3,24 to be brought after the atonement service and before the daily evening service.</i>. But some want to say, the bull, the ram, the people’s ram, and the seven unblemished sheep were brought with the daily morning offering; the remainder were brought with the daily afternoon offering. This Tanna distributes the services. What is the reason? <i>He leaves and brings his</i><i>elevation sacrifice and the people’s elevation sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:24.</i>. As mentioned in the matter. What is mentioned in the matter? His ram and the people’s ram<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">16:3,5.</i>. Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, what is the reason for the he-goat? <i>Of the blood of the atoning purification sacrifice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">This is misquoting <i>Ex.</i> 30:10 when it should be <i>Num.</i> 29:11 : <i>A he-goat as purification offering in addition to the atoning purification offering.</i> Babli 70b.</i>. The atoning purification sacrifice already preceded.
<i>Aaron comes to the tent of Meeting</i><sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:23.</i>. From where does he come? From reading the Chapter. Where does he go to? To remove the cup and the pan<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For this he has to enter the Holiest of Holies, which only is permitted in white garments.</i>. It is written: <i>He leaves and brings his elevation sacrifice and the people ’s elevation</i> sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:24.</i><i>,</i> and you are saying so? Did not Rebbi Joḥanan say, everybody agrees that the removal of cup and pan follows after the daily evening offering<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">The last action of the High Priest is mentioned in v. 24, the offering of the remaining sacrifices of the day, which includes the daily evening offering. How can one understand R. Joḥanan’s statement since v. 23, which has to be interpreted as referring to removal of cup and pan, precedes v. 24?</i>? Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, the entire Chapter is written in order except for this verse<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 70b, as Tannaitic statement.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, even this is written in order. <i>Aaron comes to the tent of Meeting.</i> From where does he come? From the first sanctification which he encounters. Where does he go to? To the last sanctification<sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">The verse only states that the High Priest has no pause in the day’s service, from the first immersion to the last washing of hands and feet.</i>. Rebbi Mana said before Rebbi Yose: Why do we not say, except the last sanctification<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">Since <i>Lev.</i> 16:23 mentions undressing the white garments but does not mention washing of any kind, whereas in vv. 4,24 it is stated that dressing requires immersion in water, would it not be logical to require washing only for dressing,. not undressing. Then R. Yose’s interpretation becomes impossible.</i>? He answered him, there is a difference since it is written <i>he comes, (he leaves)</i><sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">Since the other two quotes are from <i>Lev.</i> 16:23., this quote from v. 24 has to be deleted.</i>,<i>he takes off the linen garments.</i> <sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Chapter 3:7, Note 98.</i> Why does the verse say, <i>which he wore</i>? Could we ever think that he could undress what he was not wearing? Then why is it written, <i>which he wore</i>? It brackets undressing and dressing. Since dressing requires sanctification of hands and legs, also undressing requires sanctification of hands and legs. Rebbi Eleazar said, there is another service for which he officiates in white garments. What is it? It is removal of cup and pan<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">For this he has to enter the Holiest of Holies, which only is permitted in white garments.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan says, everybody agrees that the removal of cup and pan follows after the daily evening offering<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since in vv. 21–22 Aaron is still wearing the white garments which he put on in the morning, and in v. 24 is wearing his golden garments, there would be no reason in V. 23 to have him dress in white garments a second time. Therefore the second wearing has to be after the completion of the daily service in golden garments. The interpretation of R. Yose ben Ḥanina is the only possible one; it is compatible with the explanation which R. Yose gave to R. Mana.</i>.
Why in white clothing? Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Abba said, like the service up high so is the service down below. As up high, <i>a man in their midst clothed in linen</i><sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ez.</i> 9:2.</i>, so down below <i>a holy linen shirt he shall wear</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:4.</i>.

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> The High Priest officiates in eight garments and the common priest<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ὶδιώτης, ὁ.</i> in four, in shirt, trousers, turban, and belt<sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex.</i> 28:40,42.</i>. The High Priest in addition wears breastplate, vest, coat, and diadem<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex.</i> 28:4,36,42.</i>. In these one asks Urim and Tummim, but one does not ask for common people, only for a king, or the Court, or one on whom rest the needs of the public.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Why does the High Priest serve in eight vestments? Rebbi Ḥanania the colleague of the rabbis said, corresponding to circumcision, which takes place on the eighth day. Because of <i>My covenant was with him</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Mal.</i> 2:5. In rabbinic usage, the common meaning of “covenant” is “circumcision”.</i><i>.</i> Why does he not serve in the golden vestments? Because of haughtiness. Rebbi Simon said, because of <i>do not inflate yourself in front of the king</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Prov.</i> 25:6.</i>. Rebbi Levi said, because an accuser<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Greek κατήγωρ, κατήγορος, ὁ.</i> does not become a defense attorney<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Greek συνήγορος, ὁ.</i>. Yesterday it was written about them, <i>they made golden gods for themselves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex.</i> 32:31. Babli <i>Rosh Hashanah</i> 26a, in the name of Rav Ḥisda.</i>, and today he would officiate in golden vestments?
Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: <i>And he shall wear them</i><sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Lev<i>.</i> 16:4, based on a metathesis -לבשם בל שם .</i>, they shall be worn out there, there they were hidden, there they were rotting, and were not qualified for the next Day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli 12b and <i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i>6(7) have another argument based on <i>Lev.</i>16:23.</i>. [It was stated: Rebbi Dosa says, they were qualified for a common priest.]<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Addition of the corrector, possibly from the Babli 12b or <i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 2(1). A common priest has to wear linen shirt and trousers for the removal of ashes from the altar; <i>Lev.</i> 6:3. In addition, there is the opinion, stated later in this paragraph, that the common priest’s belt also was simply linen.</i> It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 12b and <i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 2(1).</i>: Rebbi said, there are two answers in the matter; one for clothing of the High Priest, and one for clothing of the common priest. It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 12b and <i>Sifra Ṣaw Pereq</i> 2(1).</i>: Rebbi and Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon did not disagree about the belt of the High Priest on the Day of Atonement that it was of byssus, and on all other days of the year that it contained <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">A linen belt for the Day of Atonement is prescribed in <i>Lev.</i> 16:4, while for the golden garments the belt is made from gold thread, blue, crimson, and purple wool, and linen (<i>Ex.</i> 28:5,39). The belt of common priests is mentioned as necessary in <i>Ex.</i>28:40, but its materials are not specified .</i>, about what did they disagree? About the belt of the common priest, where Rebbi says, it contained <i>kilaim,</i> but Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon says, it did not contain <i>kilaim.</i> Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: The reason of Rebbi, <i>you shall do to Aaron and his sons so, all that I had commanded you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex.</i> 29:35.</i>. Since the garments of Aaron contain <i>kilaim,</i> so also the garments of his sons contain <i>kilaim.</i> How does Rebbi Eleazar ben Rebbi Simeon explain the verse? Aaron with what is appropriate for him, and his sons with what is appropriate for them.
<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel (except for the corrector’s additions) is found in the Babli, <i>Zevaḥim</i>88b, <i>Arakhin</i> 16a, in the name of R. Anani bar Sason.</i> Rebbi Simon said, just as sacrifices atone, so the garments<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">The High Priest’s.</i> atone, shirt, trousers, turban, and vest. The shirt was atoning for [wearers of <i>kilaim</i><sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">While most of the High Priest’s garments contained <i>kilaim</i>, only the shirt was worn directly on the body. It seems that the trident here interprets the argument at the start of <i>kilaim</i> 9:1 to mean that biblically on <i>kilaim</i> which gives immediate protection to the body is forbidden.</i> There are those who want to say,]<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition (from a different source, not in the parallel in the Babli.)</i> for spillers of blood, as you are saying,<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gen.</i> 37:31.</i> <i>they dipped the shirt in blood.</i> The trousers were atoning for uncovering nakedness<sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote">The technical term for incest and adultery.</i>, as you are saying<sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex.</i> 28:42.</i>, <i>make for them linen trousers to cover the flesh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote">“Flesh” as a limb always denotes the penis, the only boneless limb.</i><i>of nakedness.</i> The turban was atoning for haughtiness, as you are saying,<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 8:9. This is a pun on the expression “thick of head” for “haughty”.</i> <i>he put the turban on his head.</i> The belt was atoning for [thieves; but some are saying, for]<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition (from a different source, not in the parallel in the Babli.)</i> the crooked ones. Rebbi Levi said, it was 32 cubits and he wound it around on both sides. The breast plate was atoning for those who bend the law, as you are saying<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 28.15.</i>, <i>you shall make a breast-plate of judgment.</i> The vest was atoning for idol worshippers, as you are saying<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Hos.</i>3:5.</i>, <i>without vest and household-gods.</i> The coat. Rebbi Simon in the name of Rebbi Jonathan of Bet-Guvrin: For two things there was no atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote">They do qualify for any obligatory sacrifice.</i> but the Torah established atonement for them. These are those: one who spreads slander, and the involuntary homicide. For him who spreads slander there was no atonement, but the Torah fixed atonement for them, the bells of the coat: <i>it shall be on Aaron in service, and its sound be heard</i><sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex.</i> 28:35.</i><i>.</i> The sound may come to atone for the sound. For the involuntary manslaughter there was no atonement but the Torah established atonement for them, the death of the High Priest<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Num.</i> 35:25.</i>. <i>He shall dwell there until the High Priest’s death</i>
It was stated: Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, atonement is mentioned inside and atonement is mentioned outside<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">Atonement is mentioned explicitly for the Temple sacrifices of the Day of Atonement, and atonement is mentioned explicitly in the ceremony of the calf whose neck is broken far away from the Temple to atone for an unexplained homicide, <i>Deut.</i>21:8.</i>. Since in atonement mentioned inside cattle atones for spillers of blood,<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">Since for the calf whose neck is broken, atonement for spilled blood is mentioned explicitly, one has to switch the references to “inside” and “outside” in this sentence. The reference to an inside sacrifice is to one of the bulls sacrificed on the Day of Atonement; it is not specified which one is meant.</i> also in atonement mentioned outside cattle atones for spillers of blood. Here unintentional, there intentional<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">The tannaitic statement of R. Eliezer ben Jacob seems to contradict the late Amoraic statement of R. Simon that the atonement for unintentional homicide was the death of the High Priest, not any sacrifice. A first answer is that R. Eliezer ben Jacob refers to intentional homicide but R. Simon to unintentional.</i>. There is a difference for the calf whose neck is broken, whether unintentional or intentional<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The previous answer is rejected, since neither the murderer nor his motives are known.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, here for known offense, there for unknown offense<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of R. Eliezer ben Jacob is rejected; only that of R. Simon is valid. It is impossible to compare the ceremony of the calf whose neck is broken to the Temple service, since in the first case it might be that the killer acted in justifiable self-defense and no sin at all was committed. But purification sacrifices are permitted only if both the fact of a sinful act and proof that it was unintentional are established.</i>.
The diadem. Some want to say, about blasphemers<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">This explanation is not in the Babli sources, Note 77.</i>. Some want to say, about insolent ones. He who says about blasphemers is understandable, since it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><i>1Sam.</i> 17:49.</i> <i>the stone sank in his forehead,</i> and it is written<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex.</i> 28:38.</i>, <i>it shall be on his forehead permanently.</i> He who says about insolent ones, <i>you had the forehead of a whoring woman</i><sup class="footnote-marker">99</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Jer.</i>3:3.</i><i>.</i>
Why are they called “Urim”? Because they were enlightening Israel<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 73b.</i>. And “Tummin”? Because they were straightening out the way before them. As long as Israel were straight, they were showing them the correct way; as we find that they lied to them in Geva Benjamin. Rebbi Jehudah said, may God forbid, they did not lie to them in Geva Benjamin. For the first time it said, “attack” but did not say “I gave”, and the second time it said, “attack” and said “I gave”<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">The references are to <i>Jud.</i> 20:23, 28 but the quotes are incorrect, the correct expressions are עלו and אתננו.</i>.
One does not ask two questions simultaneously<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 73a.</i>. If they were asked, there are Tannaim who state, He answers the first but does not answer the second; and there are Tannaim who state, He answers the second but does not answer the first; and there are Tannaim who state, He answers neither the first nor the second. He who says, He answers the first but does not answer the second; from the following: <i>David said, Eternal, God of Israel,</i> etc., <i>would the people of Qeˋilah surrender me into his hand</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ISam.</i> 23:11.</i><i>,</i> etc.? <i>]Would Saul descend,</i> David asked incorrectly. Was it not necessary, <i>would Saul descend,</i> and if he descended, <i>would the people of Qeˋilah surrender me into his hand?]</i><sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition. The question asked in 23:11 was illogical since the first question was relevant only if the answer to the second question was positive. Therefore it was necessary for David to ask the first question a second time, 1<i>Sam.</i> 23:12. There is no reason to assume a corruption of the biblical text.</i> He who said, He answers the second but does not answer the first; from the following: <i>David said, would the people of Qeˋilah surrender me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ISam.</i> 23:12..</i>, etc. He who said, He answers neither the first nor the last, <i>David asked the Eternal as follows, shall I pursue this troop, shall I reach it</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ISam.</i> 30:8.</i>? David asked for mercy in this case, <i>Eternal, God of Israel, please tell Your servant</i><sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ISam.</i> 23:11.</i>. You should know, since he asked two [questions] and He answered three [answers]: <i>He said, pursue, for catching you will catch and saving you will save</i><sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote"><i>ISam.</i> 30:8.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, he was hearing the voice; there are Tannaim who state, the writing was protruding. He who said, he was hearing the voice is understandable since it is written, <i>he heard the voice</i><sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Num</i>. 7:89.</i>. He who said, the writing<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">Identifying <i>Urim and Tummim</i> with an oracle involving the breast plate of the High Priest on whose precious stones the names of the 12 tribes were engraved, <i>Ex.</i> 28:21. This seemed to be excluded since not the entire alphabet was occurring in these names.</i> was protruding, but there is no ח for the tribes, and there is no צ [and no ק] for the tribes! Abraham, Isaac, Jacob was written on them. But there is no ט for the tribes! <i>All these are the tribes of Israel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Gen.</i> 49:28.</i> was engraved on them.

Chapter 8



Halakhah 1

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> On the Day of Atonement eating, drinking, washing, anointing, wearing shoes<sup class="footnote-marker">1</sup><i class="footnote">Leather shoes.</i>, and sexual relations, are forbidden. The king and the bride<sup class="footnote-marker">2</sup><i class="footnote">The newlywed, who should appear beautiful in her husband’s eyes, just as the king always should look handsome in public.</i> wash their heads, and the woman recovering after childbirth wears shoes, the words of Rebbi Eliezer, but the Sages forbid it.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> “On the Day of Atonement eating, drinking, etc., are forbidden.” They are punishable by extirpation and you are saying so<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup><i class="footnote">In Mishnaic terminology, “forbidden” either means forbidden rabbinically or biblically with no specified punishment. The term for severe biblical prohibitions, subject to extirpation or execution is “liable” (for a purification sacrifice in the case of inadvertent transgression.)</i>? Rebbi Hila said, it was needed for less than the minimal quantities<sup class="footnote-marker">4</sup><i class="footnote">If someone eats or drinks less than the quantities specified in Mishnah 2, the transgression is not liable for a purification sacrifice. Babli 73b.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">5</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 74b, <i>Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 7(1–4).</i>“<i>You shall deprive yourselves</i><sup class="footnote-marker">6</sup><i class="footnote">.<i>Lev.</i> 15:29.</i><i>.</i> I could think that one should sit in the sun or in the cold to make himself feel badly, the verse says, <i>do not do any work.</i> The work which I forbade you elsewhere<sup class="footnote-marker">7</sup><i class="footnote">All Sabbath prohibitions apply to the Day of Atonement.</i>. And the deprivation of what I forbade you elsewhere. Since the work which I forbade you elsewhere is work for which one would be liable to extirpation, so also deprivation which I forbade you elsewhere is deprivation from which one would be liable to extirpation, and the following are those: <i>piggul</i> and leftovers<sup class="footnote-marker">8</sup><i class="footnote">For <i>piggul,</i> cf. Chapter 5 Note 154. Eating sacrificial meat after the allotted time is sinful, <i>Lev.</i> 19:7.</i>. From where to add <i>ṭevel</i><sup class="footnote-marker">9</sup><i class="footnote">Produce from which heave and tithes have not been taken</i>? The verse says, <i>you shall deprive yourselves</i>; it added. [I am adding <i>ṭevel</i> which is a deadly sin, but would not add carcass meat which is not a deadly sin; the verse says, <i>you shall deprive yourselves;</i> it added.]<sup class="footnote-marker">10</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, necessary by the context.</i> I am adding carcass meat which is prohibited, but would not add profane meat which is not prohibited; the verse says, <i>you shall deprive yourselves</i>; it added. I am adding profane meat which is not under a commandment to be eaten, but would not add heave and Second Tithe which is under a commandment to be eaten<sup class="footnote-marker">11</sup><i class="footnote">Heave must be eaten by Cohanim in purity, and Second Tithe by the farmer’s family in Jerusalem in purity. It is sinful to let pure heave and tithes go to waste. One could have thought that food for which there is a biblical commandment that it be eaten would be exempt from the prohibition.</i>; the verse says, <i>you shall deprive yourselves</i>; it added. I am adding heave and tithe of which leftovers are not prohibited, but would not add sacrificial meat for which leftovers are prohibited, the verse says, <i>you shall deprive yourselves</i>; it added. Another explanation: <i>You shall deprive yourselves</i>, something which is a deprivation (father house) {which diminishes}<sup class="footnote-marker">12</sup><i class="footnote">The incomprehensible אב בית has been emended to עֲבֵידת (Qorban ha-Edah).</i> the spirit/ What is this? Eating and drinking. In the name of Rebbi Ismael they said, it is said here <i>you shall deprive yourselves</i>, and it says there, <i>He deprived you and made you hungry</i><sup class="footnote-marker">13</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut.</i> 8:3.</i><i>.</i> Since deprivation there is one of hunger, also deprivation here is one of hunger.”
And why six kinds? Corresponding to the six quotes of “deprivation” in the Chapter. But there are only five? Rebbi Tanḥuma said, one is with <i>musaf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">14</sup><i class="footnote">Deprivation is mentioned in <i>Lev.</i>16:29, 31 and 23:27, 29, 32. In addition it is mentioned in the paragraph on <i>musaf</i> sacrifices for the Day of Atonement, <i>Num.</i>29:7.</i><i>.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">15</sup><i class="footnote">A parallel is in <i>Taaniot</i> 1:6 (64c line 37), <b>ו</b>. The <i>Taaniot</i> text is reproduced by Tosaphoti <i>Taanit</i> 13a, s. v. אמר.</i>“Washing”. Rebbi Zeˋurah bar Ḥama, [Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Ḥanina.] in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: On a public fast day<sup class="footnote-marker">16</sup><i class="footnote">A fast day called to pray for rain in a year of drought.</i> one washes as usual his face, hands, and feet. On the Ninth of Av<sup class="footnote-marker">17</sup><i class="footnote">The fast instituted to mourn the destruction of the Temple.</i> one washes his hands and moves them over his face. On the day of Atonement one washes his hands, dries them with a towel, and moves the towel over his face. Rebbi Jonah moistened a rag and put it under the water pitcher<sup class="footnote-marker">18</sup><i class="footnote">He soaked the rag before the start of the Day of Atonement, squeezed it and let it dry somewhat during the night so that the next morning it should be somewhat moist but not dripping.</i>. But did we not state that there is no difference between a public fast and the Ninth of Av except the prohibition of work at places where they were used to it<sup class="footnote-marker">19</sup><i class="footnote">There were places where the community had adopted the rule that one did not work on the Ninth of Av. Nowhere was this extended to fast days in years of drought.</i>? If somebody was going<sup class="footnote-marker">20</sup><i class="footnote">On a day where washing was rabbinically forbidden. Babli 77b; Tosephta 4:5,</i> to his teacher or to his daughter and crossed a lake or a river he does not worry<sup class="footnote-marker">21</sup><i class="footnote">No rabbinic prohibition was violated..</i>. If his feet were dirtied he immerses them in water and does not worry. Rebbi Abba instructed following this Tanna. Rebbi Aḥa instructed that one who comes from the road and his feet are dulled, he may wash them in water. It was stated: A mourner and one in the ban<sup class="footnote-marker">22</sup><i class="footnote">Any weekday of the year, the mourner and the person in the ban are forbidden to wear leather shoes. The person in the ban has to follow these rules if he ever wants to have the ban lifted. Babli <i>Moˋed qaṭan</i> 15b.</i> on a trip are permitted leather shoes.
