When they come to the town they shall take them off. The same applies to a public fast and the Ninth of Av<sup class="footnote-marker">23</sup><i class="footnote">For everybody.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">24</sup><i class="footnote">This text is shortened from <i>Maˋaser Šeni</i>2:1 (Notes 28–35), <i>Šabbat</i> 9, Notes 122–127 and <i>Taˋanit</i> 1:6.</i>“Anointing.” As it was stated: On the Sabbath both anointing for pleasure and anointing not for pleasure are permitted. On the Day of Atonement, both anointing for pleasure and anointing not for pleasure are forbidden. On the Ninth of Av and public fasts, anointing for pleasure is forbidden but anointing not for pleasure is permitted. But it was stated: Anointing is equal to drinking regarding prohibition and reparation but not punishment<sup class="footnote-marker">25</sup><i class="footnote">Referring to illegal use of heave and dedicated food by non-Cohanim and its replacement by 5/4 of the value taken.</i>. On the Day of Atonement regarding prohibition but not punishment<sup class="footnote-marker">26</sup><i class="footnote">The only biblical prohibitions on the Day of Atonement are eating, drinking, and working. The other two, anointing and sexual relations, are rabbinic and not subject to biblical punishment.</i> But was it not stated, <i>they shall not desecrate</i><sup class="footnote-marker">27</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 22:15. The verse refers to the non-Cohen who “eats” holy food in error. Babli <i>Niddah</i> 32a.</i><i>,</i> to include him who anoints or drinks? Rebbi Joḥanan said, there is no “anoints” there. Rebbi Abba Mari said, if there is no “anoints” there is no “drinks”. For if it were not so, do matters combine which come from two different prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">28</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse in <i>Lev.</i> is needed to subsume drinking under eating, it is incomprehensible that for inadvertently eating and drinking together on the Day of Atonement one should be responsible only for one sacrifice since in that case, one infringes on two separate biblical prohibitions and should be liable for two separate sacrifices. Similarly, if one illegitimately ate and drank heave he should be liable for two separate fifths. Since in both cases the Mishnah treats eating and drinking together, the verse cannot express a separate status for drinking; the addition of anointing and drinking is rabbinic interpretation but not biblical law and there is no reason to exclude anointing.</i>?
One repeats up to “this holy body that he forgot”<sup class="footnote-marker">29</sup><i class="footnote">The body of the following is copied by the corrector who prepared the text for the Venice printer from <i>Taaniot</i> 1:6; parts of it are also quoted in other tractates.</i>.
[From where that there is a clear commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">30</sup><i class="footnote">While illegitimate use of heave oil for anointing is prohibited, it is mentioned in the framework of the farmer’s declaration in the Temple, which is a positive commandment. Overstepping the prohibition of anointing when it is forbidden legally is overstepping a positive commandment not under the scope of biblical penal law.<br>This paragraph is copied from the parallels (Note 24); there is no connection to the rules of the Day of Atonement.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Simai: <i>I did not give from it to the dead</i><sup class="footnote-marker">31</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut.</i> 26:14.</i>. Where do we hold? If not to bring a casket or shrouds for him, this is forbidden for the living, therefore certainly for the dead. What is permitted for the living but prohibited for the dead?<sup class="footnote-marker">32</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. <i>Sifry Deut.</i> 302.</i> I am saying that this is anointing<sup class="footnote-marker">33</sup><i class="footnote">Second Tithe must be consumed; no other use is authorized..</i>.
“Wearing shoes”. It was stated: In all cases where they said that one may not wear shoes, when he departs on a trip he puts them on, when he arrives at a walled city he takes them off; this includes the mourner and the person in the ban<sup class="footnote-marker">34</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Moˋed qaṭan</i> 15b. The Yerushalmi version is quoted by <i>Or zarua</i>§277.</i>. There are Tannaim who state, one goes in slippers<sup class="footnote-marker">35</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>impilia, -ium</i> (pl/) “felt slippers”.</i> on the day of Atonement; and there are Tannaim who state, one does not. Rav Ḥisda said, he who said one goes, in textile slippers, and he who said one does not go, in leather slippers<sup class="footnote-marker">36</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Yebamot</i> 12:1, Note 35, Babli <i>Yebamot</i> 102b.</i>. Rebbi Isaac bar Nahman visited Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the night of the Great Fast<sup class="footnote-marker">37</sup><i class="footnote">The day of Atonement.</i>; he came to him wearing laced shoes<sup class="footnote-marker">38</sup><i class="footnote">A leather sole with a textile upper part held together by laces.</i>. He asked him, what is this? He answered, I am asthenic<sup class="footnote-marker">39</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἀσθενής. Since the rules of not wearing leather shoes are rabbinic, they are waved for health reasons.</i>. Rebbi Samuel bar Nahman visited Rebbi Joshua ben Levi in the night of a fast day<sup class="footnote-marker">40</sup><i class="footnote">A rabbinic fast day.</i>; he came to him wearing laced shoes. He asked him, what is this? He answered, I am asthenic. They saw Rebbi Simai walking in the night of a fast day in laced shoes. A student of Rebbi Mana permitted to a relative of the Patriarch to wear laced shoes<sup class="footnote-marker">41</sup><i class="footnote">Without a medical condition which would justify waving the rules.</i>. He asked him, from where this? He answered, from this of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi. He said to him, in the case of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi it was an asthenic.
“And sexual relations.” Come and see, he is forbidden to wash, not so much more to have sexual relations<sup class="footnote-marker">42</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Day of Atonement is a day of prayer and by an institution of Ezra prayer was forbidden after sexual relations before immersion in a <i>miqweh,</i> the prohibition of washing should imply the prohibition of sexual relations without the need to spell it out.</i>? Explain it that it was a place where one does not immerse oneself, or before Ezra instituted immersion for people having had an emission. <sup class="footnote-marker">43</sup><i class="footnote">This text to the end of the paragraph is also from <i>Berakhot</i> 3:4 (Notes 167–168) which is the original source.</i> Rebbi Jacob bar Aḥa, Rebbi Yasa in the name of Rebbi Joshua ben Levi: <i>Qeri</i> is only from sexual intercourse. Rav Ḥuna said, even if he saw himself enjoying in his dream. They wanted to say, only from a woman. Rebbi Jonah and Rebbi Yose both say, even from something else. But did we not state: On the Day of Atonement eating, drinking, washing, anointing, wearing shoes, and sexual relations, are forbidden?. And it was stated in that respect: Men with <i>qeri</i> immerse themselves secretly in their normal way on the day of Atonement<sup class="footnote-marker">44</sup><i class="footnote">In Tosephta <i>Kippurim</i> 4:5 and Babli 88a the reading is: “Men with <i>qeri</i> (emission of semen) immerse themselves normally on Yom Kippur”; one speaks of a full immersion and “in secret” is not mentioned. The Tosephta is Babylonian formulation.</i>. Does this not contradict Rebbi Joshua ben Levi, since Rebbi Joshua ben Levi said, <i>qeri</i> is only from sexual intercourse? Explain it if he had intercourse on the previous day and forgot and did not immerse himself. But it was stated: It happened that one saw Rebbi Yose bar Halaphta immersing himself secretly on Yom Kippur. Can you say about that holy body that he forgot<sup class="footnote-marker">45</sup><i class="footnote">Hence, the interpretation of Rav Ḥuna is incorrect and that of rabbis Yose (the Amora) and Jonah is correct.</i>?]
“The king,” because <i>your eyes shall see the king in his beauty</i><sup class="footnote-marker">46</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is.</i> 33:17. Babli 78b, bottom.</i><i>.</i>
“The bride,” because of bad feelings<sup class="footnote-marker">47</sup><i class="footnote">That the husband should not feel bad about her. Babli 78b, bottom.</i>.
“And the woman recovering after childbirth wears shoes, the words of Rebbi Eliezer.” We were thinking to say that this refers to the last statement; it was found stated referring to all of them<sup class="footnote-marker">48</sup><i class="footnote">The entire sentence is R. Eliezer’s.</i>.

Halakhah 2

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> He who eats the volume of a big date with its pit or who drinks the volume of a full gulp<sup class="footnote-marker">49</sup><i class="footnote">Greek λυγμός, ὁ.</i> on the day of Atonement is liable<sup class="footnote-marker">50</sup><i class="footnote">Since these are biblical prohibitions, if the infraction was intentional it is a deadly sin punishable by extirpation, if in error it requires a purification sacrifice.</i>. All foods combine for the volume of a date, and all drinks combine for the mouthful. What he ate and drank does not combine.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Rebbi Yose said, this implies that (he) [one]<sup class="footnote-marker">51</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote שו, which has to be read as שֶׁהוּא “that he”. The corrector, who did not understand the Yerushalmi spelling, replaced it by prefix ש.</i> has to squeeze its empty space<sup class="footnote-marker">52</sup><i class="footnote">If the pit is loose in the date, the space between pit and flesh of the fruit is not counted in determining the volume which triggers liability.</i> since otherwise one should have stated, “it, its pit, and its empty space.” Rebbi Simeon ben Eleazar stated, a date from Nimrin<sup class="footnote-marker">53</sup><i class="footnote">In the printed version and one ms. of Tosephta 4:2 he disagrees with the anonymous Tanna there who restricts the dates considered to those growing West of the Jordan, and therefore increases the possible maximum volume of a date (Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>17:7), but in the Yerushalmi version and 2 Tosephta mss. he restricts the dates allowed as standards to just those of Nimrin and therefore reduces the permitted size.<br>Nimrin is <i>Bet Nimrah</i>(<i>Jos.</i> 13:27) in the plain East of the Jordan.</i>.
It was stated, “a full gulp.” Does this disagree<sup class="footnote-marker">54</sup><i class="footnote">In the Mishnah, the expression for solid food is “like (in the volume of) a date”, implying that pieces of food eaten at different times are added together to determine liability. The expression for fluids is “a gulp”, not “like (the volume of) a gulp”, but the statement of the Mishnah that different fluids combine show that “like (the volume of) a gulp” is understood. Is this also the position of the Tanna of the baraita or does he deny that different sips can add to a large gulp?</i>? A full gulp, what is possible as one gulp<sup class="footnote-marker">55</sup><i class="footnote">The <i>baraita</i> also agrees with the Mishnah.</i>. It was stated in the name of the House of Shammai, a full gulp is equivalent to a <i>quartarius</i><sup class="footnote-marker">56</sup><i class="footnote">A quarter of a <i>log,</i> about 133 cm<sup class="footnote-marker">3</sup>.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Ḥiyya bar Ada asked before Rebbi Mana, why was this not stated as one of the leniencies of the House of Shammai and restrictions of the House of Hillel<sup class="footnote-marker">57</sup><i class="footnote">A <i>quartarius</i> is the standard size of a cup whose contents normally are consumed in two gulps. Therefore the standard of the House of Shammai seems to be twice that of the House of Hillel.</i>? He told him, because of the gulp of Ben Avatiah<sup class="footnote-marker">58</sup><i class="footnote">He is mentioned in Mishnah <i>Kelim</i>17:12 as largest Cohen on record.<br>In contrast to the House of Shammai who determine the volume for the Day of Atonement as a standard measure, the House of Hillel insist that they vary from person to person (Mishnah <i>Kelim</i> 17:11).</i> which contained more than a <i>quartarius.</i>

Halakhah 3

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If one ate and drank in one forgetting he is liable only for one purification sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">59</sup><i class="footnote">Since not eating and drinking are subsumed under fasting, only one biblical commandment was broken.</i>. If he ate and drank, and worked, he is liable for two purification sacrifices<sup class="footnote-marker">60</sup><i class="footnote">Since fasting is commanded in <i>Lev,</i>23:27 but work is forbidden in 23:28, two distinct commandments were broken and two sacrifices are due.</i>. If he ate foods which are not edible or drank drinks which are not drinkable, drank fish sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">61</sup><i class="footnote">Fluid squeezed out of raw fish.</i> or <i>muries</i><sup class="footnote-marker">62</sup><i class="footnote">A Latin word denoting fish sauce treated with salt, water, and sometimes wine, used to dip one’s bread in but never used as a drink.</i><i>,</i> he is not liable.<br>One does not let children fast but one educates them one year of two in advance<sup class="footnote-marker">63</sup><i class="footnote">One lets girls aged 10 and boys aged 11 fast part of the day and at ages 11, respectively 12, a whole day, as education, not as biblical requirement.</i> so they should be used to commandments.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> Why? <sup class="footnote-marker">64</sup><i class="footnote">This is a partial copy, in places completed by the corrector for the Venice edition and indicated by brackets, of a text in <i>Maˋaser Šeni</i> 2:1 (Notes 7–35), also partially reproduced in <i>Ševuot</i> 3:2 (Notes 26–36). It is clear that the original is in <i>Maˋuser Šeni</i> since the last paragraph has no connection with the rules of the Day of Atonement.</i> Drinking is subsumed under eating but eating is not subsumed under drinking. From where that drinking is subsumed under eating? Rebbi Jonah understood if from the following: <i>Therefore, I told the Children of Israel, none of you shall eat blood</i><sup class="footnote-marker">65</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 17:12.</i><i>.</i> Where do we hold? If about congealed blood, was it not stated that congealed blood is neither food nor drink? But we hold, as it is. And the Torah called it eating. And was it not stated: If one liquefied fat and drank it, or congealed blood and ate it, [if there is the volume of an olive] he is liable. What does Rebbi Jonah do with this? It is not food to become impure in the impurity of food, or fluid to become impure in the impurity of fluids. Rebbi Jonah changed and understood if from the following:: <i>You shall spend the money for anything you desire</i><sup class="footnote-marker">66</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut.</i> 14:26.</i><i>.</i> Where do we hold? If about one who gives the taste of wine into a cooked dish, is that not spoiling the taste of the wine? [But we hold as it is and the Torah called it “eating.”] The rabbis of Caesarea said, explain if about <i>orzaraya</i> and <i>gomnany</i><sup class="footnote-marker">67</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Maˋaser Šeni</i> 2:1, Note 16. The first word may denote cedar resin (J. Levy) or a derivative of אוֹרֶז “rice”. The readings for the second word, גמרייה, גמזוזיניה, גומננייא show that the scribe did not know what to do with it; it may be a derivative of “gum” <i>(gummi,</i> <i>κόμμι)</i> (E. G.) used in the preparation of liquors.</i><i>,</i> since anything that is ancillary to food is like food.
Rebbi Yose understood all this from the following: “An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate and drank he is liable only once.<sup class="footnote-marker">68</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševuot</i> 3:1.</i>” The colleagues said before Rebbi Yose, but it is said following this, “an oath that I shall not eat nor drink, when he ate and drank he is liable for two”! Rebbi Yose told them, if somebody had two loaves in front of him said, an oath that I shall not eat this loaf<sup class="footnote-marker">69</sup><i class="footnote">Here a piece of text is missing, found in the other two sources: “and then he said, an oath that I shall not eat that one.”</i>, when he ate [both of them] together would he not be guilty on two counts?
Rebbi Ḥanania in the name of Rebbi Phineas understood it from the following: “An oath that I shall not eat; when he ate inedible food and drank undrinkable fluids, he is not liable.<sup class="footnote-marker">70</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševuot</i> 3: 5.</i>” Because he ate inedible food and drank undrinkable fluids. Therefore if [he ate edible food,]<sup class="footnote-marker">71</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition, added in error.</i> drank drinkable fluids he is liable. Did he not say, an oath that I shall not eat? That is understandable following our Mishnah, where we are saying, “an oath that I shall not eat.” But for Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">72</sup><i class="footnote">Obviously since Rebbi is the editor of the Mishnah, he cannot contradict his own text. With the text in <i>Ševuot</i> one has to read: “those rabbis who say, an oath that I shall not eat and I shall not drink.” These rabbis are quoted in the Babli, <i>Ševuot</i> 23a.</i> who said, an oath that I shall not eat and shall not drink?
Rebbi Ḥinena understood it from the following<sup class="footnote-marker">73</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah 3.</i>: [“If he ate and drank in one forgetting he is liable only once.”
Rebbi Abba Mari understood it from the following:] <i>I did not eat from it in my mourning,</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut.</i> 26:14.</i> but I drank?
It is understandable for him who said, “{I swear} an oath that I shall not eat”, and he drank. Drinking is subsumed under eating. But for him who said, “an oath that I shall not drink”, and he ate? Is eating subsumed under drinking? Eating is not subsumed under drinking<sup class="footnote-marker">75</sup><i class="footnote">The preceding arguments show that drinking is subsumed under eating. Nobody holds that a mention of drink includes solid food. Babli <i>Ševuot</i> 23a.</i>.
Some want to understand it from this: <i>You may not eat in your gates</i><sup class="footnote-marker">76</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut.</i> 12:17.</i> etc. <i>Your cider’,</i> that is the wine. <i>And your shining oil’</i> refers to anointing and the Torah called it ‘eating’. But this is not clear. If you could say that it is clear one should be whipped because of it outside the walls<sup class="footnote-marker">77</sup><i class="footnote">If the verse were a formal identification of anointing and eating as far as Second Tithe is concerned, the use of impure heave oil for anointing should be a criminal offense.</i>! Rebbi Yose ben Ḥanina said, one is whipped outside the walls only for pure Second Tithe which entered Jerusalem and left. From where that it is not clear? From what was stated: “On the Sabbath, both anointing for pleasure”, one reads this in Halakhah One<sup class="footnote-marker">78</sup><i class="footnote">Notes 24 ff.</i>.
From where that it is clear as a positive commandment<sup class="footnote-marker">79</sup><i class="footnote">While illegitimate use of Second Tithe oil for anointing is prohibited, it is mentioned in the context of the farmer’s declaration in the Temple, which is a positive commandment. Therefore overstepping the prohibition is violating a positive commandment not under the scope of criminal law.</i>? Rebbi Eleazar in the name of Rebbi Simai: <i>Nor did I give from it to the dead</i><sup class="footnote-marker">74</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut.</i> 26:14.</i><i>.</i> Where do we hold? If it were to bring a casket and shrouds for him, that were also forbidden for a living person<sup class="footnote-marker">80</sup><i class="footnote">Since only consuming Second Tithe is permitted.</i>! If something is forbidden for the living, not so much more for the dead? What is something which is permitted for the living but prohibited for the dead? That is anointing!
<sup class="footnote-marker">81</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Sifra Emor Pereq</i> 14(3). Babli 81 a.</i>“Warning about work on the day: <i>Any work you shall not do</i><sup class="footnote-marker">82</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:29.</i><i>.</i> Punishment, <i>and I shall destroy this person</i><sup class="footnote-marker">83</sup><i class="footnote"><i>.Lev.</i> 23:30.</i><i>.</i> Warning about deprivation on the day, <i>for any person who will not be deprived</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 23:29.</i><i>;</i> punishment, <i>and this person will be extirpated</i><sup class="footnote-marker">84</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 23:29.</i><i>.”</i> There is no warning about work in the night, there is no punishment. There is no warning nor punishment written for deprivation in the night<sup class="footnote-marker">85</sup><i class="footnote">In both verses quoted from <i>Lev.</i> 23 it is stressed בְּעֶ֖צֶם הַיּ֣וֹם הַזֶּ֑ה which in general in interpreted “in full daylight” (<i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael Bo</i> 9.) Since the night is forbidden as is the day (<i>Lev.</i> 23:32), there is an obvious contradiction to be resolved.</i>.
Rebbi Simeon ben Laqish asked, what could He have said instead of <i>will not be deprived?</i> “Shall not eat”? All eating in the Torah is by the volume of an olive, but here by the volume of a date<sup class="footnote-marker">86</sup><i class="footnote">Since it was established earlier that of the six kinds of deprivation required on the Day of Atonement only not fasting is biblically punishable, the question arises why instead of the generic expression “be deprived” the verse does not directly prescribe “do not eat.” The answer is that in the latter case already eating the volume of an olive would be punishable.</i>. Rav Hoshaia said, “watch yourself, lest, not be deprived,” would be three warnings<sup class="footnote-marker">87</sup><i class="footnote">This now addresses the problem from where could one infer that all prohibitions of the Day of Atonement also apply to the preceding night. He suggests that since there are several expressions biblically used to indicate prohibitions, there could have been different expressions used for the warnings which would have been interpreted as applying to different times.</i>. Rebbi Ḥuna said, watch yourself regarding the saying which I said to you, <i>watch yourself in matters of skin disease to be very careful and act</i><sup class="footnote-marker">88</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Deut.</i> 24:8. This is a side remark noting that הִשָּׁמֵר is used only in reference to rules spelled out on other occasions. In this particular case it is noted that the rules of the impurity of skin disease (<i>Lev.</i> 13–14) do not include prohibitions; the prohibition implied by <i>Deut.</i> 24:8 is interpreted (cf. Pseudo-Jonathan <i>ad loc.</i>) to mean that it is forbidden to eliminate the impurity of skin disease by surgically eliminating the diseased tissue.</i><i>,</i> etc. Rebbi Ḥiyya stated: Punishment could [not]<sup class="footnote-marker">89</sup><i class="footnote">Necessary addition by the corrector.</i> have been said about work since it is unnecessary. I could have inferred it from deprivation. [Since for deprivation]<sup class="footnote-marker">90</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition which seems to be unnecessary.</i> which is minor one is liable to extirpation, for work which is major<sup class="footnote-marker">91</sup><i class="footnote">“Major” means that prohibitions of work are frequent, applying to the Sabbath as capital crimes and to holidays as misdemeanors; deprivation is “minor” since it applies only to the day of Atonement.</i> it should be logical that one should be liable for extirpation. Therefore punishment was spelled out for work only to imply a warning preceding it<sup class="footnote-marker">92</sup><i class="footnote">The argument of Rav Hoshaia is essentially correct but has to be realized differently. Since the warning spelled out for work on the Day of Atonement is shown to be unnecessary, it has to be applied as if it were written twice; supplying the desired additional warning to be read as referring to the preceding night.</i>. Since the punishment indicated for work is preceded by a warning, also the punishment indicated for deprivation is preceded by a warning. Rebbi Zeˋira said, this implies that one may infer about an “equal cut” even if it is free only on one side<sup class="footnote-marker">93</sup><i class="footnote">He reads the argument of R. Ḥiyya not as a conclusion <i>de minore ad majus</i> but as “equal cut” meaning that the expression בְּעֶ֖צֶם הַיּ֣וֹם הַזֶּ֑ה appearing in <i>Lev.</i> 23:29,30 implies that the rules indicated in both verses are the same. In general one requires that an expression used for “equal cut” not be used for any other inference; but here the entire v. 29 is needed both for warning and punishment (Note 84). There remains the problem that since now it is shown that בְּעֶ֖צֶם הַיּ֣וֹם הַזֶּ֑ה does not mean “during daylight only”, the expression is not used for any other deduction and therefore the “equal cut” is between two identical expressions not otherwise needed and therefore valid according to everybody, even R. Ismael. One has to say that since the day is defined in <i>Lev.</i> 23:32 as from evening to evening, בְּעֶ֖צֶם הַיּ֣וֹם הַזֶּ֑ה means that while one has to start fasting and refraining from work somewhat earlier and finish sometime after nightfall the next day, the biblical penalties do not apply to these additional periods and therefore one mention of “on this very day” is needed for this legal implication.<br>Noted as opinion in dispute, Babli <i>Šabbat</i> 64a, <i>Niddah</i> 22b.</i>. Rebbi Yudan said, Is that not Rebbi Aqiba’s? And Rebbi Aqiba said, one infers about an “equal cut” even if it is not free<sup class="footnote-marker">94</sup><i class="footnote">The Babli would reject the entire argument of R. Zeˋira since it permits the use of “equal cut” only based on an oral tradition. It still would recognize R. Ḥiyya’s argument but it categorically rejects extending the scope of punishments by hermeneutical arguments.</i>.
It was stated: Rebbi Eliezer ben Jacob says, it is said <i>on this very day</i> about work and it is said <i>on this very day</i> about deprivation. Since in regard of <i>on this very day</i> said about work you did not differentiate between day and night<sup class="footnote-marker">95</sup><i class="footnote">As argued by R. Ḥiyya.</i>, between punishment and warning<sup class="footnote-marker">96</sup><i class="footnote">Which are written in one and the same verse.</i>, so in regard of <i>on this very day</i> said about deprivation we shall not differentiate between day and night, between punishment and warning. So far following Rebbi Aqiba.
Following Rebbi Ismael? <i>It shall be for you an eternal law, in the Seventh Month</i><sup class="footnote-marker">97</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:29.</i> He joined work and deprivation. Since the work which I forbade to you is work for which one is liable to extirpation, also deprivation which I forbade to you is deprivation for which one is liable to extirpation<sup class="footnote-marker">98</sup><i class="footnote">As required by Rav Hoshaia.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">100</sup><i class="footnote">The main source of this paragraph are <i>Terumot</i> 6:1, Notes 16–19, <i>Šabbat</i> 14, Notes 83–89.. Babli <i>Yoma</i> 80b/81a.</i> Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, he who eats of <i>kilaim</i> in a vineyard<sup class="footnote-marker">101</sup><i class="footnote">Any non-vine produce in a vineyard is prohibited for usufruct.</i> is whipped<sup class="footnote-marker">102</sup><i class="footnote">If there are witnesses to the act, since he broke a biblical commandment. This corresponds to the term “is liable” used for Sabbath violations.</i>. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, he who chews wheat grain<sup class="footnote-marker">103</sup><i class="footnote">A pure Cohen who is entitled to eat heave may not spit out the grain after chewing.</i> of heave is whipped. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan, he who sips vinegar of heave is whipped; he who sips vinegar of heave pays the principal but he does not pay the fifth<sup class="footnote-marker">104</sup><i class="footnote">The fifth computed from the top which is a quarter from the bottom, due for use of heave by people not entitled to its use.</i>; he who chews wheat grain of heave pays the principal but he does not pay the fifth. Rebbi says, I am saying that he pays principal and fifth. Rebbi Jeremiah in the name of Rebbi Immi: The rabbis agree with Rebbi about one who sips heave vinegar from his dipping that he pays principal and fifth since vinegar refreshes<sup class="footnote-marker">105</sup><i class="footnote">Even though vinegar in itself is neither food nor drink, when it was absorbed by bread it becomes food and stays food. For heave it remains subject to the fine, on the Day of Atonement it remains forbidden drink, but on the Šabbath it may be used for a toothache.</i>.
“One does not let children fast on the Day of Atonement.” Rav Ḥuna explained the Mishnah: One does not let children fast on the Day of Atonement, and does not train them<sup class="footnote-marker">106</sup><i class="footnote">One tells them to fast part of the day.</i>, but one or two years before one trains them<sup class="footnote-marker">107</sup><i class="footnote">One does not let minor children fast the entire day.</i>. Rebbi Joḥanan explained the Mishnah: One does not let children fast on the Day of Atonement, but one does train them<sup class="footnote-marker">108</sup><i class="footnote">Younger than 10 for girls and 11 for boys.</i>, and one or two years before they complete the fast. Until when? Rebbi Aḥa, Rebbi Ḥinena, Rebbi Jacob bar Idi in the name of Rebbi Simeon ben Halaphta: At age nine or age ten<sup class="footnote-marker">109</sup><i class="footnote">According to the Babli 82a age nine refers to healthy children and age ten to sickly ones. Since the Yerushalmi gives no indication of this one might read this as 9 for girls, 10 for boys, in each case 3 years before they become obligated to fast as adults</i>.

Halakhah 4

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A pregnant woman who smelled [food]<sup class="footnote-marker">110</sup><i class="footnote">She gets an urgent desire for the food and if she continues to fast there would be a danger of a miscarriage.</i> one feeds until she is well again. A sick person one feeds according to experts<sup class="footnote-marker">111</sup><i class="footnote">Medical personnel.</i>; if there are no experts one feeds him on his own judgment until he says, enough<sup class="footnote-marker">112</sup><i class="footnote">Even though fasting is a biblical commandment and breaking the fast is a deadly sin, biblical obligations never should endanger one’s life (<i>Lev.</i> 18:5)</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> At first one dips it in sauce<sup class="footnote-marker">113</sup><i class="footnote">One dips kosher bread in the non-kosher sauce which may be forbidden only rabbinically.</i>. If she feels well again it is good. Otherwise one gives her of the body of forbidden food<sup class="footnote-marker">114</sup><i class="footnote">If the woman smells cooking on the day of Atonement, probably it is Gentile non-kosher food. But see the paragraph after the next.</i>. Two pregnant women came before Rebbi Tarphon. He sent two students to them and told them, go and tell them, it is the Great Fast-day. They said it to the first one and she quieted down. They said about him<sup class="footnote-marker">115</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is the baby who carves the food, not his mother who simply is the provider. For similar stories Babli 82b/83a.</i>, <i>from my mother’s</i><i>womb You are my God</i><sup class="footnote-marker">116</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps</i>. 22:11.</i>. They said it to the second, who did not quiet down. They said about him, <i>The wicked are perverts from the womb; they err from the belly, the speakers of lies</i><sup class="footnote-marker">117</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps.</i>58:4</i>!
<sup class="footnote-marker">119</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 83a.</i> The sick person says, I can<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He can fast the entire day.</i>, but the physician says, he cannot; one listens to the physician. The physician says, he can, but the sick person says, he cannot; one listens to the sick person<sup class="footnote-marker">120</sup><i class="footnote">He can fast the entire day.</i>. The only question is if the sick person says, I can, and the physician says, I do not know. Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: It is treated as an uncertainty of a danger to life, and any uncertainty of a danger to life pushes the Sabbath aside<sup class="footnote-marker">121</sup><i class="footnote">Since the principle is established for the Sabbath, the violation of which is a capital crime, it certainly holds for the day of Atonement, whose violation is a deadly sin.</i>.

Halakhah 5

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A person suffering from boulimy<sup class="footnote-marker">132</sup><i class="footnote">Greek βουλιμία. ἡ. “ravenous hunger”. It is presumed that the attack is life threatening.</i> one feeds even impure food until his eyes light up. One does not feed the cover of a rabid dog’s liver to a person bitten by a rabid dog<sup class="footnote-marker">133</sup><i class="footnote">Since this is not a recognized medical procedure.</i>, but Rebbi Matthew ben Ḥarash permits it<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">For him it is proven medical practice</i>. In addition, Rebbi Matthew ben Ḥarash said, if somebody suffers from throat ache, one pours him medicine into it since it is an uncertainty of a danger to life, since any uncertainty of a danger to life pushes the Šabbath aside.<br>If a wall collapsed over somebody<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath or the day of Atonement.</i>, it is uncertain whether he is there or not, whether he is alive or dead, whether he is a Gentile or a Jew, one digs him out<sup class="footnote-marker">136</sup><i class="footnote">Even though this involves a Sabbath desecration.</i>. If one finds him alive, one digs him out, if dead one shall leave him<sup class="footnote-marker">137</sup><i class="footnote">Until after the end of Sabbath or holiday</i>.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">122</sup><i class="footnote">This paragraph should belong to the next Halakhah, discussion of Mishnah 5.</i> From where that one may feed him fig cake and raisins of heave? From this: <i>They gave him a slice of fig cake and two raisins</i><sup class="footnote-marker">123</sup><i class="footnote"><i>1Sam.</i> 30:12. Since the verse reports that the Egyptian recovered from having been without food for 72 hours, this sugary food is proven antidote to boulimy and may be given even from prohibited food.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Ḥuna said, the twenty-four <i>esronot</i> which David ate, he ate in hunger<sup class="footnote-marker">124</sup><i class="footnote">He implies that Ahimelekh gave to David all shew breads of the Tabernacle (<i>1Sam.</i> 21:7) and he ate all of them. There are 12 breads, each of them 2 <i>esronot</i>(<i>Lev.</i>24:5). 24 <i>esronot</i> are 2.4 <i>epha,</i> or 7.2 <i>seah,</i> a volume of about 92 liter of flour. (Cf. S. Abramson, Sinai 63(1968), pp. 193–197.)</i>. This happened to Rebbi Joḥanan<sup class="footnote-marker">125</sup><i class="footnote">An attack of boulimy.</i> and he ate everything which happened to be before him. They quoted about him, <i>wisdom revives its possessor</i><sup class="footnote-marker">126</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Eccl</i>. 7:12.</i>. It was stated: “One feeds him the least forbidden<sup class="footnote-marker">127</sup><i class="footnote">If no kosher food is available at the moment. Babli 83a, Tosephta 4:4.</i>. Carcass meat or heave, one feeds him heave. <i>Ṭevel</i> and sabbatical produce<sup class="footnote-marker">128</sup><i class="footnote">While illegitimate use of heave oil for anointing is prohibited, it is mentioned in the framework of the farmer’s declaration in the Temple, which is a positive commandment. Overstepping the prohibition of anointing when it is forbidden legally is overstepping a positive commandment not under the scope of biblical penal law.</i>, one feeds him Šabbatical produce.” <i>Ḥallah</i> and <i>ˋorlah</i> is problematic<sup class="footnote-marker">129</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ḥallah</i> as heave is a sanctum, forbidden to non-Cohanim. Its improper ingestion is a deadly sin. Usufruct of <i>ˋorlah</i> is forbidden to everybody as a simple prohibition.</i>. Rebbi Yose said, I questioned before Rebbi Ba: Heave is a deadly sin, carcass meat a prohibition<sup class="footnote-marker">130</sup><i class="footnote">Eating non-kosher meat is a simple prohibition punishable by 39 lashes.</i>, and you are saying so? Following him who said, they accepted the tithes voluntarily<sup class="footnote-marker">131</sup><i class="footnote">In this opinion, tithes, heave, and Sabbatical produce are biblical obligations only if all 12 tribes are on their ancestral lands; today all these obligations were voluntarily accepted by the returnees from Babylon and have only rabbinic status.; <i>Ševiˋit</i> 6:1, Notes 11–13. But non-kosher food is biblically forbidden.</i>..
The characteristics<sup class="footnote-marker">138</sup><i class="footnote">Greek σημεῖον, τό.</i> of a rabid dog: His mouth is (closed) [open]<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The text is brackets is the corrector’s, corresponding to the text in the Babli, 83b.</i>, his spittle drips, his ears are badly smelling, he tail is between his hips, he walks at an angle<sup class="footnote-marker">140</sup><i class="footnote">His body makes a non-zero angle with the direction of his movement.</i>, the dogs bark at him, and some are saying, also he barks but his voice is not (carried) [heard]<sup class="footnote-marker">139</sup><i class="footnote">The text is brackets is the corrector’s, corresponding to the text in the Babli, 83b.</i>.
What is the cause of a dog being rabid? Rav and Samuel. One is saying, a spirit of madness came over him. But the other one said, a woman uses him to try out witchcraft. German, the slave of Rebbi Yudan the Patriarch, was bitten by a rabid dog and he fed him the cover of its liver, but he never recovered. A person could never tell you that he was bitten by a rabid dog and survived, that he was stung by a brightly colored snake<sup class="footnote-marker">141</sup><i class="footnote">A sign of a poisonous reptile.</i> and survived, that he was kicked by a mule and survived, but only by a white mule<sup class="footnote-marker">142</sup><i class="footnote">Babli <i>Ḥulin</i>7b; Yerushalmi <i>Berakhot</i>8:6, Notes 124–125.</i>
From where that any uncertainty of a danger to life pushes the Sabbath aside? Rebbi Abbahu in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: <i>Only you shall keep My Sabbaths</i>;<sup class="footnote-marker">143</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 31:12.</i> a diminution. Some want to say, the Torah said,<sup class="footnote-marker">144</sup><i class="footnote">Since אַךְ everywhere is taken as sign of a diminution, but there is no indication of what is excluded from the observation of the Sabbath, the rabbinic authorities are empowered to determine the exclusion by an argument. Babli 85b, <i>Mekhilta dR. Ismael, Ki Tlssa.</i></i> desecrate the Sabbath for him, so he will dwell and keep many Sabbaths.
Rebbi Zeˋira, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: If a wall collapsed in a dead-end street populated by Gentiles, but where one Jew lived, one digs there because of the Jew<sup class="footnote-marker">145</sup><i class="footnote">Also quoted in <i>Ketubot</i> 1:10, Note 269.</i>. How far<sup class="footnote-marker">146</sup><i class="footnote">Since in the Mishnah it is stated that a live person has to be dug out completely, the question is how much of a person has to be uncovered in order to determine his death.</i>? Two Amoraim. One said, up to his nose; the other said, up to his navel. He who said, up to his nose, about one who was healthy; he who said up to his navel, about one who was intoxicated<sup class="footnote-marker">147</sup><i class="footnote">Reading רוי for רבי.</i>.
It was stated: (The one who decided is praiseworthy, one who is asked has to be ready,)<sup class="footnote-marker">148</sup><i class="footnote">The text in parentheses is the scribe’s. It was not understood by the corrector who partially substituted the Babli text.<br>The original text states that the rabbi who decided in advance to instruct his congregation to act immediately in cases of danger to life is praiseworthy, the one who is asked anyhow has to have his instructions ready without hesitation, and the one who wastes time to ask is a murderer.</i> [the one who is quick is praiseworthy, the one who is asked is blameworthy,]<sup class="footnote-marker">149</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s text. The first statement is from the Babli, 84b (Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i>15:11). The second clause is difficult to understand; what can a person do if he is asked? He is blameworthy if he does not cut the interrogator short and sends him to help others.</i> and the one who asks is a spiller of blood.
<sup class="footnote-marker">150</sup><i class="footnote">This text also is in <i>Nedarim</i> 4:9 Notes 104–113. <i>Šabbat</i> 16, Notes 123–132; partially Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i> 15:15, Babli, <i>Yoma</i> 84b.</i> It was stated: In any case of danger to life one does not say that the work be done by Gentiles or minors, but even by adult Jewish persons. “It happened that there was a fire in the courtyard of Yose ben Simai in Shihin, and the garrison of the barracks<sup class="footnote-marker">151</sup><i class="footnote">Latin <i>castra</i>, <i>-orum,</i> n. “military camp, barracks, fortress.”</i> of Sepphoris came to fight it but he did not let them fight it; he said, let the collector collect his due<sup class="footnote-marker">152</sup><i class="footnote">Since the fire was on a Sabbath, he took it as divine punishment.</i>. Immediately there formed a cloud which extinguished it. After the Sabbath he sent to each of them a tetradrachma and to their commander<sup class="footnote-marker">153</sup><i class="footnote">Greek ἔπαρχος, ὁ, equivalent of Latin <i>praefectus (castrorum).</i> Tosephta <i>Šabbat</i>13:9 (ed. Liebermann).</i> 50 denarii.” The Sages<sup class="footnote-marker">134</sup><i class="footnote">For him it is proven medical practice</i> said, there was no need for him to do that. A Nabatean was a neighbor of Rebbi Jonah. There was a fire in Rebbi Jonah’s neighborhood; the Nabatean wanted to fight it but Rebbi Jonah did not let him. He said to him, because of my property! He said, yes<sup class="footnote-marker">135</sup><i class="footnote">On the Sabbath or the day of Atonement.</i>. And everything was saved. Rebbi Jonah from Kefar-Immi spread his garment over the burning grain stack and the fire retreated from it.

Halakhah 6

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> A purification sacrifice and a definitive reparation sacrifice<sup class="footnote-marker">156</sup><i class="footnote">Any reparation sacrifice (אָשָׁם) except the one for the suspicion of a sin which, if proven, would require a purification sacrifice; <i>Lev.</i> 5:17–19.</i> atone<sup class="footnote-marker">157</sup><i class="footnote">Since it is presumed that an impurity which requires a reparation sacrifice is divine punishment for some infraction, atonement applies also to these sacrifices.</i>. Death or the Day of Atonement atone if accompanied by repentance. Repentance atones for minor infractions, positive commandments, and prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical prohibitions for which no punishment is indicated in the text.</i>. But for severe ones<sup class="footnote-marker">159</sup><i class="footnote">Sins punishable by extirpation or the death penalty.</i> it suspends until the next Day of Atonement arrives and atones.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> There we stated: <sup class="footnote-marker">160</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Ševuot</i> 1:9.</i> Intentional [impurity]<sup class="footnote-marker">161</sup><i class="footnote">Word missing in the quote.</i> of the Sanctuary and its <i>sancta</i> is atoned by the he-goat whose blood is brought inside<sup class="footnote-marker">162</sup><i class="footnote">On the Day of Atonement.</i> and the Day of Atonement. The remainder of the transgressions mentioned in the Torah, minor or severe ones, intentional and unintentional, [known and unknown, positive commandments and prohibitions, extirpations and capital crimes, the scapegoat atones.]<sup class="footnote-marker">163</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s completion from the Mishnah.</i>
<sup class="footnote-marker">164</sup><i class="footnote">The text of the Halakhah is from <i>Ševuot</i> 1:9, Notes 183–210. The present paragraph also in the Babli <i>Ševuot</i> 12b.</i> Are not minor sins positive commandments and prohibitions; are not serious ones extirpations and capital crimes? Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">165</sup><i class="footnote">With the <i>Ševuot</i> text, read “Rav”.</i> Jehudah said, so is the Mishnah: “Minor or serious ones. Those minor ones, whether he committed them intentionally or did not commit them intentionally<sup class="footnote-marker">166</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer either to infractions for which the penalty is not spelled out (Note 158) or to cases where guilt cannot be ascertained. The standard example is that of a person who ate one of two pieces of meat, one of which was kosher, the other one severely forbidden either as forbidden fat or as sacrificial meat· which became impure (Rashi). If it is not possible to ascertain which of the two he ate. then a suspended reparation sacrifice is due; but the obligation to bring a suspended reparation sacrifice is cancelled by the Day of Atonement, as determined in the next paragraph (and Ševuot Halakhah 1:2, Note 69, Horaiot 1:1 Note 18).</i>. Those severe ones, whether he obtained knowledge of them or did not obtain knowledge of them<sup class="footnote-marker">167</sup><i class="footnote">This refers to the situation described in the previous Note. If he intended to eat one of the two, knowing that one was severely forbidden, he either committed no sin or he committed a serious crime for which no personal sacrifice can atone.</i>. The following are minor sins: positive commandments and prohibitions<sup class="footnote-marker">158</sup><i class="footnote">Biblical prohibitions for which no punishment is indicated in the text.</i>. Severe ones, extirpations and capital crimes. Just as the he-goat whose blood is brought inside atones for intentional infractions and suspends for the unintentional ones in cases where a sacrifice is required, also the scapegoat atones in the same way<sup class="footnote-marker">168</sup><i class="footnote">Which carries away “all their iniquities”, <i>Lev.</i> 16:22). This last statement is missing in the Babli <i>Ševuot;</i> it is discussed in <i>Keritut</i> 25b.</i>.”
It is understandable if it did not come to his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">169</sup><i class="footnote">Then no private sacrifice is due and the public sacrifice must atone for the damage done to the sanctuary.</i>. If it did come to his knowledge<sup class="footnote-marker">170</sup><i class="footnote">Did we not imply that the public offering does not relieve the individual of his obligation to bring a sacrifice?</i>? Was it not stated:<sup class="footnote-marker">171</sup><i class="footnote">Parts of Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 6:4. Purification sacrifices must be brought even after the Day of Atonement but obligations of suspended reparation sacrifices are eliminated.</i> “From where that those liable for purification sacrifices and certain reparation sacrifices for whom the Day of Atonement had passed, remain liable, but those liable for suspended reparation offerings are no longer liable?” Rebbi Abun bar Ḥiyya said, whether it was known to him on it, or not known to him on it<sup class="footnote-marker">172</sup><i class="footnote">The text from <i>Ševuot</i> is copied here very incompletely, only partially completed by the corrector’s addition [in brackets]. By the omission the text has become unintelligible; cf. <i>Ševuot</i> 1:9, Notes 194–196.</i>. [Why did he not say, if it was not known to him]. His words imply that even if it was not known to him on the Day of Atonement, the Day of Atonement atones.
There<sup class="footnote-marker">173</sup><i class="footnote">Mishnah <i>Keritut</i> 1:2.</i>, we have stated: “For these<sup class="footnote-marker">174</sup><i class="footnote">The transgressions punishable by extirpation enumerated in Mishnah <i>Keritut</i>1:1.</i> one is liable for extirpation in case of intentional transgression, or a purification sacrifice for unintentional transgression, and for what was not certainly known in their cases a suspended sacrifice.” But did not the day of Atonement already atone<sup class="footnote-marker">175</sup><i class="footnote">Does the Day of Atonement not eliminate the possibility of a suspended reparation offering for the possibility of a sin committed before this day?</i>? Rebbi Simeon in the name of Rebbi Levi from Sokho: The Mishnah refers to one who rebels on the Day of Atonement.
A positive commandment, even if he did not repent. A prohibition? Rebbi Samuel in the name of Rebbi Zeˋira, only if he repented<sup class="footnote-marker">176</sup><i class="footnote">While the Mishnah in Rav Jehudah’s interpretation treats positive commandments and simple prohibitions in parallel, there is a difference between the two kinds of sins. The non-performance of a positive commandment is atoned for even without repentance while the atoning for breaching simple prohibitions requires repentance.</i>. If one said, “the elevation offering does not atone,<sup class="footnote-marker">177</sup><i class="footnote">The biblical text does not indicate for which kind of sin an elevation offering does atone but <i>Lev.</i> 1:4 indicates that it atones. The next paragraph will investigate for which sins it is atoning.</i>” does the elevation offering not atone? It atones even against his will. But if he said, “it is impossible that the elevation offering atone for me,” it does not atone against his will<sup class="footnote-marker">178</sup><i class="footnote">In the prior formulation, it was simply a false statement. But if somebody said, I am opting out, the atoning power of sacrifices shall not be valid for me, what he offers would be profane. If there is no offering, there cannot be atonement.</i>. If one said, “the Day of Atonement does not atone,” It atones. “I cannot accept<sup class="footnote-marker">179</sup><i class="footnote">The scribe wrote איפשי, “it is impossible for me”, which Galilean form the corrector did not recognize (and which the scribe himself in the preceding sentence exchanged for the Babylonian form), and added אי, for Babylonian אִי אֶפְשִׁי, meaning the same.</i> that it atone for me,” it atones against his will<sup class="footnote-marker">180</sup><i class="footnote">Since he brings the offering on his own initiative, if it is not brought for atoning it does not atone. But the Day of Atonement is given by God; it is not up to man to say what it can or cannot do. Babli, <i>Keritut</i> 7a.</i>. Rebbi Ḥanina ben Rebbi Hillel said, it is not up to a person to tell the King, “you are no king.”

Halakhah 7

<strong>MISHNAH:</strong> If somebody say, “I shall sin and repent, I shall sin and repent,<sup class="footnote-marker">190</sup><i class="footnote">Repeatedly</i>” one<sup class="footnote-marker">191</sup><i class="footnote">Heaven</i> does not let him achieve to repent. “I shall sin and the day of Atonement will atone,” the Day of Atonement does not atone. Transgressions between a person and the Omnipresent the Day of Atonement atones; but those between him and his neighbor the Day of Atonement does not atone unless he placated his neighbor.<br>The following did Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah explain: <i>Of all your unintentional sins before the Eternal you shall be cleansed</i><sup class="footnote-marker">199</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:30. The sentence is not parsed as <i>from all your iniquities, before the Eternal you will be cleansed,</i> but as <i>from all your iniquities before the Eternal, you will be cleansed. Sifra Aḥare Pereq</i> 8:1.</i><i>.</i> Transgressions between a person and the Omnipresent the Day of Atonement atones; but those between him and his neighbor the Day of Atonement does not atone unless he placated his neighbor.<br>Rebbi Aqiba said, blessed are you, Israel. Before whom do you cleanse yourselves, and who cleanses you? Your Father Who is in Heaven, as it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">200</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ez</i> 36:25.</i>, <i>I shall sprinkle on you pure water, so you shall be pure.</i> And it is said<sup class="footnote-marker">201</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Jer.</i> 17:13.</i> <i>Israel’s hope is the Eternal,</i> since a <i>miqweh</i> purifies the impure people<sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Identifying מקוה “hope” or “ritual bath; pond”.</i>, so the Holy One, praise to Him, cleanses Israel.
<strong>HALAKHAH:</strong> <sup class="footnote-marker">181</sup><i class="footnote">In <i>Lev. rabba</i> 7(3) the paragraph is reproduced and the doctrine is attributed to R. Simeon ben Iohai.</i> The elevation offering atones for thoughts. What is the reason? <i>What rises in your spirits will not be</i><sup class="footnote-marker">182</sup><i class="footnote">Ez. 20:32.</i><i>.</i> Rebbi Levi said, <i>What rises in your spirits will not be</i><sup class="footnote-marker">183</sup><i class="footnote">This quote is incorrect and makes no sense. The original is in <i>Ševuot</i> 1:9 Note 205.</i><i>.</i> And so <i>Job</i> says, <i>he got up early in the morning and brought elevation offerings in the number of all of them, etc.</i><sup class="footnote-marker">184</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Job</i> 1:5. Job offered elevation offerings since he was afraid that his children might have had impious thoughts.</i> [This implies that the elevation offering atones for thoughts.<sup class="footnote-marker">185</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from Š<i>evuot.</i></i>]
<sup class="footnote-marker">186</sup><i class="footnote">This must refer either to infractions for which the penalty is not spelled out (Note 158) or to cases where guilt cannot be ascertained. The standard example is that of a person who ate one of two pieces of meat, one of which was kosher, the other one severely forbidden either as forbidden fat or as sacrificial meat which became impure (Rashi). If it is not possible to ascertain which of the two he ate, then a suspended reparation sacrifice is due; but the obligation to bring a suspended reparation sacrifice is cancelled by the Day of Atonement, as determined in the next paragraph (and <i>Ševuot</i> Halakhah 1:2, Note 69, <i>Horaiot</i> 1:1 Note 18).</i> Rebbi says, the Day of Atonement atones for all sins against the Torah except for him who tears away the yoke, or who breaks the Covenant, or who finds aspects in the Torah<sup class="footnote-marker">187</sup><i class="footnote">This was explained in <i>Peah</i> 1:1 Notes 199–213; cf. also <i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1, Note 8. One who tears away the yoke is he who recognizes the authority of the Torah but decides to break its laws; one who breaks the covenant is he who tries to reconstruct a prepuce and does not circumcise his sons; one who finds aspects in the Torah is he who denies the divine origin of the Torah.</i>, where it atones if he repented but does not atone otherwise. Rebbi Zevida said that Rebbi Yasa asked: Does Rebbi think that the Day of Atonement atones without repentance? Rebbi Ashian, Rebbi Jonah, Rebbi Abba, Rebbi Ḥiyya in the name of Rebbi Joḥanan: Rebbi agrees that the Day of Atonement only atones with repentance; therefore death cleanses without repentance<sup class="footnote-marker">188</sup><i class="footnote">This is a non-sequitur, but it fits well with the text in <i>Ševuot</i> which has to be considered as the original. The Babli supports the statement there about the Day of Atonement; it has no statement of Rebbi about the power of Death.</i>. We have stated thus: The day of death equals the day of repentance. Who stated this? Rebbi! Then what we stated, “death and the Day of Atonement atone with repentance,” does not follow Rebbi<sup class="footnote-marker">189</sup><i class="footnote">Since the Mishnah requires repentance also on the Day of Atonement. Rebbi formulated an anonymous Mishnah, i. e., authoritative doctrine, following the majority against his own opinion.</i>.
<sup class="footnote-marker">192</sup><i class="footnote">From here to the end of the Halakhah, the text is not only in <i>Ševuot</i> but also in<i>Sanhedrin</i> 10:1, Notes 23–37, 14–22 (and in the Babli <i>Yoma</i> 86b). For part of the text there exists a Geniza fragment (G) edited by L. Ginzberg in his <i>Yerushalmi Fragments</i> (1909) p. 267.</i> Rebbi Matthew ben Ḥarash asked Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah in the Academy<sup class="footnote-marker">193</sup><i class="footnote">He asks for an answer on the record.</i>. He said to him, did you hear the four types of Atonement which Rebbi Ismael explained? He answered him, there are three in addition to repentance. One verse says, <i>return, naughty children</i><sup class="footnote-marker">194</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Jer.</i> 3:22.</i><i>.</i> But another verse says, <i>for on that day He shall atone for you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">195</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev.</i> 16:20.</i><i>.</i> And another verse says, <i>I shall visit their crime with the rod</i><sup class="footnote-marker">196</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps.</i> 89:33</i><i>,</i> etc. And another verse says, <i>the iniquity of this people shall not be atoned for until you die</i><sup class="footnote-marker">197</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Is.</i> 22:14.</i><i>.</i> How is this? If somebody violates a positive commandment and repents, before he moves from there it will be forgiven to him. About this one it says, <i>return, naughty children.</i> If one transgresses a prohibition and immediately repents, repentance suspends judgment, and the Day of Atonement atones. About this one it says, <i>for on that day, He shall atone for you.</i> If one intentionally transgressed {sins punishable by} extirpations or death penalties, repentance and the Day of Atonement atone half, and sufferings during the other days of the year atone half. About this one it says, <i>I shall visit their crime with the rod, and their iniquities with plagues.</i> But by whom the Name of Heaven was desecrated, there is no power in repentance to suspend judgment, nor in the Day of Atonement to pardon, nor in sufferings to scour; but repentance and the Day of Atonement atone one third, sufferings atone a third, and death scours with sufferings. About this one it says, <i>the iniquity of this people shall not be atoned for until you die.</i> From this we learn that death scours.
Rebbi Joḥanan said, these are the words of Rebbi Eleazar ben Azariah, Rebbi Ismael, and Rebbi Aqiba. But the Sages say that the scapegoat atones. How does it atone? Rebbi Zeˋira said, in small pieces. Rebbi Ḥanania said, at the end. What is the difference between them? If somebody died in-between. In the opinion of Rebbi Zeˋira, he already was pardoned. In the opinion of Rebbi Ḥanina, he was not pardoned. Rebbi Zeˋira said, a <i>baraita</i> supports Rebbi Ḥanina: “There is strength in the ram which is not in the Day of Atonement, and in the Day of Atonement which is not in the ram: The Day of Atonement pardons without a ram, but the ram does not atone without the Day of Atonement. There is strength in the ram since the ram atones immediately but the Day of Atonement only atones at nightfall.<sup class="footnote-marker">198</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 4:16.</i>” Rebbi Ḥuna said, the question was raised before Rebbi Jeremiah and he said, explain it if they intended to bring another he-goat but they did not bring it. Rebbi Yose ben Rebbi Abun asked, does the Holy One, praise to Him, not see into the future? Then He should pardon immediately
<sup class="footnote-marker">203</sup><i class="footnote">Cf. Babli 87a</i> Samuel said, a person who sinned against his neighbor has to tell him, I offended you. If he accepts it, it is good. Otherwise he brings people and appeases him in their presence. That is what is written<sup class="footnote-marker">204</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Job</i>33:27</i>, <i>being straight with men,</i> he shall form a row of men, <i>and say, I sinned, and straightforward I acted criminally, and I was careless.</i> If he acts in this manner, the verse says about him<sup class="footnote-marker">205</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Job</i>33:28</i>, <i>He delivered his soul from perdition, and his life enjoys light.</i> If he died, one has to appease him on his grave and say, I offended you.
<sup class="footnote-marker">206</sup><i class="footnote">Babli 86b; Tosephta 4:15.</i> It was stated: “Sins which he confessed the past Day of Atonement he does not have to confess the next day of Atonement; if he would do this, the verse says about him, <i>like a dog returning to his vomit, so is the silly repeating his foolishness</i><sup class="footnote-marker">207</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Prov.</i> 26:11.</i><i>.</i> It was stated, Rebbi Eliezer<sup class="footnote-marker">208</sup><i class="footnote">In Babli and Tosephta: R. Eliezer ben Jacob.</i> says, he would be clever and be rewarded. What is Rebbi Eliezer’s reason? <i>But my sin is always before me</i><sup class="footnote-marker">209</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ps</i>. 51:5.</i><i>.”</i> How does Rebbi Eliezer interpret the rabbis’ reason, <i>like a dog returning to his vomit, so is the silly repeating his foolishness?</i> If he repeats the same sin. How do the rabbis interpret Rebbi Eliezer’s reason, <i>but my sin is always before me?</i> It should not be in his eyes as if he never did it, but that he did it and he was forgiven.
<sup class="footnote-marker">210</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 4:14, Babli 87b.</i>“It is an obligation to confess on the eve of the Day of Atonement when it gets dark, before he immerses himself in eating and drinking<sup class="footnote-marker">211</sup><i class="footnote">The text is elliptic; it is understandable only in the Babylonian sources. Since confession is one of the obligations of the day of Atonement, one has to confess immediately after nightfall in the evening prayers (as presupposed in the following sentence.) But as a precaution there is a rabbinic obligation to confess in the preceding afternoon, during afternoon prayers, before taking the big meal before the fast, in case one would be incapacitated during the fast-day because of overeating.</i>. Even though he confessed in the evening, he has to confess in the morning; though he confessed in the morning, he has to confess at <i>musaf</i><sup class="footnote-marker">212</sup><i class="footnote">The additional holiday prayers, parallel to the additional sacrifices required on such days. It is spelled out in the Babylonian sources that all confessions are appended to the prayers of the day.</i><i>;</i> though he confessed at <i>musaf,</i> he has to confess in the afternoon; though he confessed in the afternoon, he has to confess at <i>neˋilah</i><sup class="footnote-marker">213</sup><i class="footnote">The additional prayers on fast days (Mishnah <i>Taˋaniot</i> 4:1), shortly before the end of the fast.</i><i>,</i> for the entire day is qualified for confession.” How does he confess? <sup class="footnote-marker">214</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Lev. rabba</i> 3(3), ascribed to another author.</i> Rebbi Berekhiah in the name of Rebbi Abba bar Binah: My Master, I sinned and did evil, and I was involved in bad thoughts, and acting in aberrant ways. But as I acted I shall not act. May it please You, Eternal, my God, [that You will atone for all my crimes]<sup class="footnote-marker">215</sup><i class="footnote">Corrector’s addition from <i>Lev. rabba.</i></i>, forgive me for all my sins, and pardon (and forgive)<sup class="footnote-marker">216</sup><i class="footnote">Text of the scribe, deleted by the corrector. Since with this word and with the prior addition by the corrector one also has three requests for forgiveness, the original text of the scribe looks genuine.</i> all my transgressions.
It was stated<sup class="footnote-marker">217</sup><i class="footnote">Tosephta 4:14, Babli 86b, Yerushalmi <i>Nedarim</i> 5:4, Note 60.</i>: It is necessary to detail one’s actions, the words of Rebbi Jehudah ben Bathyra. Rebbi Aqiba says, one does not have to detail his actions. What is Rebbi Jehudah’s reason? <i>Please, this people committed a grave sin, they made themselves golden idols</i><sup class="footnote-marker">218</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ex</i>. 32:31.</i><i>.</i> How does Rebbi Aqiba treat this? Who caused it to them? I, for I gave them much silver and gold. Why? For a donkey is not braying<sup class="footnote-marker">219</sup><i class="footnote">The corrector’s spelling נהק is biblical <i>(Job</i> 6:5, 30:7), but the scribe’s spelling נקק may be the Aramaic form of Accadic <i>nagāgum..</i> While in <i>Job</i> the verb describes the shout of the hungry, here it is used for the noise of the overbearing; cf. <i>Sifry Deut.</i>308</i> out of a basket of carob fruit<sup class="footnote-marker">220</sup><i class="footnote">In the Babli, <i>Berakhot</i> 32a, the argument is that a lion will shout eating a box of meat.</i>.
It is written, <i>Israel’s miqweh is the Eternal.</i> Just as a <i>miqweh</i><sup class="footnote-marker">202</sup><i class="footnote">Identifying מקוה “hope” or “ritual bath; pond”.</i> purifies the impure, so the Holy One, praise to Him, purifies Israel. And so He says, <i>I shall sprinkle on you pure water and you will be pure; from all your impurities and your abominations I shall purify you</i><sup class="footnote-marker">221</sup><i class="footnote"><i>Ez.</i> 36:25</i><i>.</i>
